"THE BULLETIN.”
(published at SYDNEY, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly-Ausiralin's Nouparell
Illustruted Newsper.
“The Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis-
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A-Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Vote.
Complete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians.—The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, aim the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be. a
State monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “ nobility. ’
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter that is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
by the countless newspaper-ventures in which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent
large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by com par. ng
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent sonif
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has over «■
The Bulletin is the only well established us-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It {mints with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress : it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of halt the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the damns of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, aeainst the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushmau of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Au tralia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

i THE DUKE AND DUCHESS OF

TO THE EDITOR 07 THE DAILT TEX. BCR A PH.
Sir, —May I ask for a space in your columns
•for a suggestion which 1 make With all
respect in regard to the visit of the Duke
and Duchess of York. It is that Australia
should now ask that our soil should be thd
birthplace of the young prince. If this
could be granted I venture to predict a
greater consolidation of the Empire than all
the schemes that have yet been propounded.
—Yours, etc.,
J. W. M-CDTCHEON, Major.

SATURDAY, JANUARY 13, 1894.
K.C.M.G.
The recent list of New Year “honours”
forms only an unappreciable addition to the
innumerable knights of Australasia, but none
the less it is, in a small way, another aggra-
vation of an already aggravated evil. The
latest “knights ” are mostly peaceful medio-
crities who are not likely to drag their
decorations through the police and insolvency
courts, as other ennobbled Australasians have
lately done, and that is probably the very
best that can be said for them. Poor,
ordinary, commonplace Jenkin Coles is pro-
bably about the most absolutely barren per-
sonage who ever had greatness thrust upon
him; and Manning, M.L.A., of Sydney, is
remarkable chiefly as a man who looks nice
in a dress-suit and as the author of an his-
toric grovel to a departed Governor. The
prospective elevation of Dr. Ren wick, of
Sydney, is chiefly remarkable because it
promises to adorn the Order of St. Michael
and St. George with an even flatter and
more utterly desolate individual than
.Ienkin Coles, and in this and other
respects Renwick will be worthy of the
Order, and the Order will be in every way
worthy of him. And several other persons,
whose knighthoods are looming in the near
future, will bo fit company for Renwick, for
the list of Australasian knights is like the
geography of the moon, and consists mostly
of a catalogue of dead craters standing use-
lessly among their own ashes.
• * •
Some day it will be interesting to read the
complete history of K.C.M.G.-ism in Aus-
tralasia, and see how patiently and persist-
ently the curse of the ancient butcher
of Cappadocia has followed the Order. The
list of K.C:M.G.’s is mostly a dismal record
of mediocrity, failure, disaster, general
wreck, and dishonoured old age. Graham
Berry was the typical man of the Order,
lie resigned the position of Tribune of the
People for his bauble, and now the same
Berry, aged 74 or thereabouts, feeble, poor,
broken-spirited and discredited, is seen
perspiring and hustling to make his way into
a tenth-rate banquet at a small Victorian
town, and when he attempts to utter a few
uncertain platitudes he is howled down
by the impatient mob. Berry’s title got
him a position on the directorate of the
Mercantile Bank, and the Mercantile
cleaned him out almost to the last coin.
Julius Vogel faded away almost exactly
in the same fashion as Berry, and his
latter years are just as hopeless and a
degree more impecunious. Pakkes looked
as if he was on his way to greatness when he
was blasted by a decoration, and now he is only
known as a tricky patriarch of many finan-
cial troubles, and many unprintable stories—
a hopeless old man of strange shifts and
expedients, and the most colossal failure in
Australian history. Dirbs got his title
and promptly took it into bankruptcy.
So did poor, weak, pompous Benjamin
Benjamin, of Melbourne. Matthew Davies
took his title into the Police Court instead,
and may presently take it into the dock so
that it may be cleaned and repaired before a
higher tribunal. M‘Oulloch, Griffith,
MTlwraith, Stout —but the list is too long
for recapitulation. The knights of Aus-
tralia are becoming as thick as leaves on
Vallombrosa, and they are, almost without
exception, a dreary catalogue of failures,
dull mediocrities and dishonoured renegades.
# * #
The knights that are to come are much the
same as those that have already arrived.
James Monro, unfortunately, was overtaken
by disaster before his title reached him, and
missed his opportunity. The circumstance
is much to be regretted, for the profoundly
serious old man with his two broken banks
and his queer building society, and his
melancholy umbrella, would have been a
congenial ornament to the Order. But the
ultimate elevation of Duncan Gillies is
almost certain, and that narrow, ignorant
schemer, with the oily smile and the shiny
countenance, will be another bright example
of the sort of merit which meets with im-
perial recognition. Gillies is the apotheosis
of the Commonplace—in fact, it is difficult
to imagine how any one man could contrive
to preserve such a dead level of intelligence
and such an utter dull-brownness of soul as
he has done for many years, and never have
even one bright or novel idea to relieve the
prevailing monotony. J. B. Patterson, the
blatant meat-individual, is obviously a coming
knight; and the elevation of the rotund
and blastiferous Bent is not impossible.
Looking down the long, dreary list of
Australian politicians, it is always possible to
select a dozen or two who, if they do not die
within a given time, or get into gaol, or
become insolvent under especially ugly cir-
cumstances, or be too publicly and con-
spicuously drunk or immoral, must inevitably
reach the distinction of a place in the Order
of St. Michael and St. George. The first
requisite is to be loyal and reasonably grovel-
sorne; the second is to be elderly, old-
established, commonplace, and profoundly
ordinary in every respect.
* • •
It is a notable fact that the miry ways of
politics are practically the only road to
titular distinction in Australasia. A man
may be great in any other walk of life, but
unless he is an old, case-hardened placeman
there is no K.C.M.G. -ship on his horizon.
The local nobility is a gathering of the
“old Parliamentary hands” of these pro-
vinces, and their wives—and nothing more.
Why the wife of the ennobbled one should,
in these days of women’s rights and separate
matrimonial estates, share his brand and
ear-mark and degradation is not easily
explained. If he has done anything great
or npble in politics, or anything especially
crawlsome or disgusting, or if he has earned
his distinction by being especially common
or ordinary in a public capacity for many
years, she has had no share in the matter.
Therefore, the Australian “lady,” like all
the rest of her kind, is merely an irrelevant
accident, somewhat after the fashion of the
traditional Texan “colonel,” who owes his
dignity to the fact that he married the widow
of another colonel deceased, the latter having
either earned his title while still in the flesh,
or having obtained it as a matter of courtesy.
Why the person who marries the widow of
a defunct K.C.M.G. does not, by an inver-
sion of the present rule, thereby become a
K.C.M.G. himself, is another matter which
is difficult of explanation. The much-
married K.C.M.G. may give his alleged
dignity to three or more wives in succession,
and there seems no coherent reason why his
relict, who has an equally lawful claim on
her end of the “dignity,” should not transfer
it to one or two or more husbands in like
manner.
• * #
By-and-bye, when the political woman
becomes a Parliamentary institution, the
question of titles will assume anew aspect.
It will be necessary then to ennobble the pro-
minent Parliamentary female—the masterful
personage who has stonewalled persistently
in a mortified-looking brown bonnet, and
has helped to form Cabinets and to overset
Ministries, and has slept in the House, with
her feet and her brown stockings on the
seat, and her reticule full of Parliamentary
papers under her head, when a falling
Government is holding the fort against
heavy odds, and has led forlorn hopes in
unfriendly constituencies against obloquy
and fierce opposition and eggs that have seen
their best days. Then it will be necessary
to consider whether the political woman shall
be made a knight and become Sir Sarah,
in which case her husband, who, in the usual
course of events, will boa meek nonentity
much given to lurking humbly in far corners,
will presumably be a “lady;” or- whether
she shall be made a knight, and her husband
be allowed to remain as he is ; or whether
her title, being in the female line, shall
reflect, not on her husband but on her
grandmother ; or whether she shall be made
a “lady” on her own account; or whether
her husband, or, in default of a husband,
her nearest male relative, shall be “elevated ’
to the rank of K.C.M.G., and she be
simply allowed to shine in his reflected gor-
geousness.
• * #
These are a few of the possibilities of the
Australian “peerage.” Meanwhile, knights
continue to multiply exceedingly and their
“ladies” grow so numerous that when a
woman of mental power and originality arises
in Australia she will presently petition
to be elevated to the rank of “Mrs.” in
order to be aloof from the ruck. Even if
the alleged decoration is only one degree
higher than the insignia of a jam-pot sus-
pended on a string, it grows oppressive when
it becomes too numerous, just as the honour
of being King Billy fell into contempt when
every male-member of the Australian abori-
ginal race began to lay claim to an elabor-
ately-engraven brass half-moon of his own.
Australia is fast drifting into that abject state
in which everybody is either King Billy or
his gin.
The Contingent Vote: Again
Editor Bulletin, Dibbs’ motives are, of
course, open to question, btit he is right in say-
ing the Contingent Vote would utterly smash
Labour. When it is remembered that there are
smug citizens all over the land who have fat-
tened by devotions at the shrine of Thrift
hoping to enter Parliament on the town-pump
vote, and on a scattering of the suffrages of the
more wavering supporters of Plutocracy and
Democracy, it cannot be denied that the Con-
tingent Vote system is an excellent device for
the promotion of their designs ; and when it is
considered that the interests of Capitalism, as
represented by the Pastoralists’ Association, the
Employers’ Union, the National Association,
and other like bodies throughout Australia, find
that their power to effectively combine against
the interests of the masses in the direct war
between Labour and Capital does not extend
to the political arena, and when they, from
this cause, fail to prevent two, three,
and even more would-be politicians, openly
or secretly of their social opinions, from
running in opposition to one Labour champion,
it is not to be wondered at if the Contingent
Vote system, which would enable them to back
the whole of their candidates and to obtain for,
at least, the more plausible of these, a share of
the Labour vote, should have ardentand actively-
interested partisans.
• ' # *
Such being the case, it is not surprising that
the present writer’s article, condemning the
Contingent Vote, should have provoked
responses from irate correspondents. My
critic, “F.R.,” virtually admits my chiefest
assertions—that men of pronounced opinions
would lose by the Contingent Vote. Thus says
“F.R. ”: “It is true that the Contingent
Vote, if generally used, would be disastrous to
* extremists,’ ” and forthwith he proceeds to
say who these would be. They would be “ the
Wingen Abbotts,” the “temperance hog,” the
man who wants to run the State for the sole sake
of “ Bung,” the advocate of general confisca-
tion of property, and the “ frenzist ” of every
cause, be it Labour, Capital, Drink or Social
Purity. These would all be left in the “ lurch,”
and that, he continues, is “just the virtue of
the Contingent Vote.”
* * #
Quite so, only that is precisely why the Con-
tingent Vote is the Vole of Stagnation, and
why it may be correctly described as the
apotheosis of mediocrity ; though with this
qualification—that the temperance fanatics, the
free-liquor advocates, and' the Social Purity
men, however frenzied some of them may be,
are in no place very numerous, and though
thousands may believe in prohibition, many in
free-liquor, and some m State puritanism, it is
safe to assert that in no part of the coun-
try will even a respectable minority of any
of these be found who will sink every
other consideration for the sake of their fad.
As things are, in the politics of Australia,
there is no man in Parliament who sits there,
solely, on the anti-beer, pro-beer, or pro-Bible
vote. Woman-suffrage, alone, is capable of
such a phenomenon. Like all similar suffrages,
the temperance-vote is divided every time
between the great contending factions of the
State; and, according to his taste, a strict
temperance elector may consistently support a
prohibitionist Freetrader, a prohibitionist Pro-
tectionist or a prohibitionist Social Democrat;
but the body of voters in any constituency who
would ukase that they wanted a candidate with
no principles but anti-beer ones, no ideas but
free-liquor ones, or no thoughts but Social
Gimlet ones, is scarcely to be found within the
limits of the State. Thus, these and all
similar sections of the community may be dis-
missed from this subject.
* « •
But how different is the case when you come
to the great crucial question which threatens
to absorb all others—the great issue between
the haves and the have-nots ; and, again, in a
less degree, between the rival fiscal factions !
Everywhere there are hosts of men who are for
Capitalism and class-rule, and masses of them
who are for Labour, and in either case
these will subordinate their opinions on
those subjects to no other consideration
whatsoever. Similarly, were the social
war non-existent, the rival fiscal partisans
would, swamping all minor issues, divide the
country between them, and do so, even now,
despite the Labour war, when the local circum-
stances give them a monopoly of the chances.
But, in face of tlie common foe of both
in the face of Labour—it is only too true,
“ F.R.” to the contrary notwithstanding, that
the rival fiscal factions cun make a truce for
the moment in order to assail the more success-
fully the deadly foe of both. Witness, for this,
the conduct of the Freetrade press of Sydney
in supporting, at several critical conjunctures,
the Protectionist administration when it was
denounced and attacked on behalf of the masses.
Only the other day a Freetrade candidate for a
suburban electorate was ypsited on by a deputa-
tion, representing the Protectionists of the
locality, who assured him that, Protection
being under a cloud, the adherents of
that policy had determined to risk no
forlorn hope, and would solidly support him
at the forthcoming'elections. The significance
of this fact lies in the circumstance that a
Labour candidate is in the field. What the
writer contends for in this connection is not that
there are not Labour Freetraders and Labour
Protectionists, but that, where the Democratic
sympathy of these is thorough, they will, in
all cases, give their primary vote to the cause
which is greatest, and when they see, as they
have seen on quite a number of occasions, the
rival journals and politicians of fiscaldom
standing shoulder to shoulder with the National
Association.To_.“ down ” Labour, it- cannot be
doubled that the cause which thus solidly
unites for its destruction the contending fac-
tions pf_dQaiiasicvatism. is. tlie.uia.user which is
greatest, the cause which is dearest to true
democrats of all fiscal tendencies.
* • »
It is considerations of this kind which make
it emphatically true that if, in the forthcoming
battle, the struggle be sometimes between
Freetrade and Protection, it will more often be
between Labour and the combined forces of
fiscalism, and, under present circumstances, a
multiplicity of candidates will by no means
obscure the issue, but merely give victory to
the side which is best disciplined ; whilst, in
every cose, the strong man, the firm man, who
is true to his party, be it Labour or Capitalism,,
will have the best chance. But different by
far would be the result of the Contingent Vote,,
for then not the strong man, nor the firm man,
but the middle man, the little-of-Labour indi-
vidual and the good-deal-of-Capi'tal personage
would come greatly to the front, to his honour
and profit, but little to the glory or gain of the
State.
# # *
Better a Parliament of M'Millans and!
Scheys than one of fat aldermen and in-
tellectual vacuity. Better the daring and*
domination of the Mountain than the hesitations
and stagnation of the Plain. Better men with,
faith and ideas, even if they be bad, than pur-
poseless creatures with no aspirations beyond;
their own comforts; yet, if you force on us the.-
Contingent Vote system, these last will arise
on the suffrages of uncertainty and intellectuals
vacuity, and stagnation and selfishness will be;
cast on our backs for three soul-killing years.
* * *
Thus far with “F.R.,” and now a word to-
“C.H.S.,” another critic who challenges the
statement that Griffith was the author, in
politics, of the Contingent Vole. The reply to
this is that Griffith was practically Dictator
of the Parliament which passed the Contingent
Vote, and, as the responsible head of
the Government, lie, and he alone, is.
accountable for the measure one, too,,
which rightly belonged to the Chief
Secretary’s Department. And even if Bar-
low, the Lands Minister, had anything
to do with the drafting of the Bill, the man who.
believes Griffith, the best Parliamentary
draftsman in the politics of Australia, would!
suffer a subordinate to frame and put through,
the Legislature, without the aid and concur-
rence of his chief, a measure of such importance-
as the Contingent instrument, is gifted with
more faith than is necessary for this age. —R.T..
A Bewildering- Verdict.
The dreary ninety days Victorian libel-ease of
Speight v. Syme was concluded last week.
Twelve jurymen, whose collective feebleness of
intellect became so obvious towards the finish
that the J udgp waxed satirical at their expense,
found a verdict which is a gross injustice to one
or other of the contending parties. Whether
the jury was more hazy than the Law, or the
Law was less hazy than Judge Hodges’ ex-
planation of it, are matters of general uncer-
tainty. There can be no intelligent doubt con-
cerning the illogical apportionment of damages
to the ex-Commissioner of Railways. Mr.
Speight charged the Age with having pub-
lished eleven distinct libels upon his personal
character, or business capacity, in the course of
a prolonged agitation for his dismissal from
office. The said agitation being a chief part
and parcel of the Age’s political policy—being,
in, fact a virtual.command from Davld Syme to
his vassal, Premier Shiels —resultedinSPEiGHT’s
ruin. Unlike the ninety-nine in every hundred
plaintiff's who sue newspapers for damages,
Speight had ample cause for complaint, unless
the Age could prove right up to the hilt the
truth and justice of the accusations which laid,
him low.
* # •
Speight was the hundredth individual who-
does indeed suffer real damage—not, be it re-
membered, from a chance paragraph, but from
a long and systematic attack with large type.
A libel-action, or the threat of one, at least,
was inevitable even before the Age had ceased
to attack and Speight had ceased to exist as
Railway Commissioner. That Speight, thrown
out of his billet and encumbered with private
liabilities, would ever succeed in fighting the
wealthy newspaper to a finish, was not antici-
pated. Awful legal obstacles were put in his
way in the hope that he would die of financial
starvation ere he could drag his case to court.
By the time Speight got there his grievance
was nothing if not substantial. A very differ-
ent case to that ,of the typical blackmailer of
the libel-court.
* * •
After Richard Speight had gone into the
witness-box—where his accusers said he would
not dare to go—and done his best to discredit
the Age charges—after counsel had said their
contradictory says and the learned Judge had
thrown a misty veil of lore over the merits of
this remarkable case, the jury retired to con-
sider its verdict, it transpired that one **£


the fateful twelve (and he probably spoke for
others beside himself) was troubled by an ina-
bility to remember all the points that he was
told to bear in mind. After two or three days’
consideration, the foreman positively could not
say for certain what decisions they had come to
upon each of the eleven counts, and he asked for
another two or three days in which to find out
the true position of affairs. Ultimately, the
Jurymen confirmed their first statement that they
could agree only upon one matter. Nine or
more of them considered that the Age was wrong
in stating that £250,000 worth of railway stores
had been stored unnecessarily, and were
deteriorated down to a value of £50,000, “ thus
showing a clear loss of £200,000.” This state-
ment they found to be, firstly a libel, secondly
an untruth, and thirdly not a fair comment.
On the remaining ten libel counts the jury
disagreed. The Age was in no instance sup-
ported by nine men, whereas in most cases
there were eight votes for Speight, it is said.
Under these circumstances the fixing of the
damages at £lOO looks like a concession by
eight of Speight’s supporters to one of his
opponents, who may have refused to be the
necessary ninth man unless they would pass the
paltry sum mentioned. It is incredible that
those who were with Speight on all, or nearly
all, of the counts would estimate the damage
effected by this particular libel at £lOO. If so,
they are very melancholy illustrations of
special-jury intelligence. On the other hand,
if the Age had a majority, or a bare
six supporters, upon all or most of
the ten disputed counts, then by all
common sense rules, Speight did not deserve
more than the orthodox farthing, which should
reward a victory that is legal, rather than
moral. Either has Speight been denied fair
compensation, or David Syme is robbed of the
“ moral victory ” he had a right to claim—and
does claim, as it is, without attempting to prove
his right. A farthing damages verdict would
have done that for him. The £lOO verdict
merely excites wonder as to the average opinion
of the muddle-headed jury, said wonder being
no greater than the certainty that their opinion
is worth very little anyway,
• # #
How, in the name of stupidity, did they
arrive at such an assessment ? A three-fourths
majority having decided in Speight’s favour
upon the one libel, the jury would naturally be
supposed to vote their individual estimates of
damages (after, perhaps, the majority had
agreed as to a maximum amount to be voted),
and divide the sum total by twelve, a plan
which gives the minority a show to reduce the
average by voting nil. Each juror would ex-
press his views on the whole case by the amount
he was prepared to award on the one count.
The result would be the same as if Speight
had confined himself to the particular libel that
lay in a plain question of fact. The jurymen did
not seize their one opportunity to act rationally.
J udge Hodges seemed to be at some pains not
to direct them so to do. He loaded them up
with Law, and the Law, which is always a
roguish hass, is such an especial hass in libel-
cases that the twelve—being a feeblecrowd easily
mastered by the animal—became mentally in-
competent to follow their inclinations.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Salvation in Fixed Dates.
The virtue of the death penalty as a tonic for
true repentance has often been suggested in
The Bulletin, but until last week no Christian
clergyman had openly supported the opinion of
this journal concerning gallows regeneration.
It remained for a cheerful Presbyterian, Rev.
A. J. Campbell, to go straight out for The
Bulletin doctrine in a letter to Melbourne
Argus. Mr. Campbell’s immediate purpose
was to promote the hanging of Mrs. Knorr, a
lady who ought, as it appears, to revel in her
doom, seeing that St. Paul once observed : “If
I have committed anything worthy of death I
refuse not to die.” That Scriptural quotation
alone sufficed to settle the reprieve question,
for it proved that if Paul had been a
female baby-farmer of the back-yard-burial
type he would not have declined to
fall through a platform at 10 a. mi. Mrs.
Knorr’s execution having received the official
sanction of religion, so to speak, Rev. Camp-
bell proceeded to show where the beauty of it
came in:—“ Indeed,” said the thoughtful
shepherd, “I am not sure that one who has been
doing great wickedness is not in a more favour-
able position for turning from it to God, and
seeking pardon at the Cross, when death is
certain on a day fixed and near at hand than
when it is postponed through a life-long im-
prisonment.” Mr. Campbell may feel quite
certain on this point. If salvation consists in
feeling much comforted by ghostly advice as
“ the hour ” approacheth there is no church on
earth to compare with the condemned cell for
saving influence. Life-long imprisonment is
not at all an infallible cure for sin, judging
from the people who try small or medium doses
of the remedy. “ Certain death on a day
uxed and near at hand ” is the best thing for
Mrs. Knorr’s soul. By the same token it must
be a most desirable finish for hundreds of other
Victorians whose wickedness has been con-
siderable, if not exactly great. And, inasmuch
as Rev. Campbell runs the risk of becoming a
backslider and a lost one himself (for no man
can guarantee his own godliness to be fireproof)'
then the State would do well to hang the
rev. gentleman upon the third Sunday of next
month, not because he is wicked, but in order
to make the assurance of such a good man’s
safety doubly sure.
Kanaka Politicians.
liie wrath of God has been to some extent
averted from Queensland by the supplications
cf several suffering sects ; and, as usual, some-
body else meanly steps in to take the credit of
the better prospects vouchsafed by PROVIDENCE
to that afflicted province. Sydney Telegraph
has interviewed Attorney-General Byrnes and
Minister for Lands Barlow, and each modestly
poses as the saviour of his country from the
horrors of white labour, just electoral, repre-
sentation, and the laud for the people. One
has a grey head ou green shoulders, the other a
green head on grey shoulders, but their impu-
dence is just about equal. Byrnes —to whom,
as a native of Queensland, there is the
greater damnation—extols the cheap Kanaka,
and says that every one at work means
work for a white man. This Is the
old, old lie. Byrnes knows that on
any plantation in Queensland —pick where he
will from Bundaberg to Cairns —it is rare to
find ten whites to a hundred Kanakas, mill-
hands included. The labour indirectly em-
ployed certainly won’t account for the other
90 per cent. Further, Byrnes’ apologia pro
Kanaka suo evades the point, which is this :
Is Queensland to become a community of half-
caste mongrels, with every white child depen-
dent for a living on the importation of a
Kanaka child to find work for it ? Byrnes
says it is. “ Coloured labour is popular to an
immense degree. No party would ever dare to
interfere with it.” This is a new lie, and The
Bulletin knows, if Byrnes does not, that
Queensland will some day—and soon—prove it
a new libel. Mr. Barlow chiefly dodders con-
cerning the contingent vote, a nightmare which a
perverse and sinful Queensland generation has
handed down to posterity as “ Barlow’s folly.”
It is time this abortion were forgotten until a
Government arises with the sense to strangle
it. Intended to stifle the democratic vote, its
chief effect was to relegate a whilom Minister
to obscurity. As this individual had cheerfully
included support of the contingent vote among
other prostitutions of his talents for the sake
of place and pelf, “Barlow’s folly” has not
been wholly in vain in the eyes of the Queens-
land democracy.
A “ Revenue Basis ” —for Freetrade.
The Argus is just now rabidly anxious to
increase the Vic. revenue by lowering all those
Customs duties which have checked importa-
tion and reduced Government supplies. The
Freetrader is, for the moment, a patriot, inas-
much as he would willingly shed every drop of
ink in his bottle to save the public exchequer.
For the Argus there is no possible, way of
attaining the desired surplus except by cutting
down protective duties and inducing a glorious
inflow of goods from Europe. A land-tax
would “ kill the farmers,” an income-tax would
“ prove ineffectual,” because nobody has an
income to put the tax upon, and, since things
are bad all round, Victoria’s last chance lies in
the proposed slaughter of the local manufac-
turer. There is something howlingly funny in
the Argus plea for putting “ the tariff substan-
tially upon a revenue basis.” The notion of a
country being so impecunious that it can only
manage to pay current expenses by procuring
its necessaries from a foreign shop is sugges-
tive of Gilbert and Sullivan opera. But the
average Victorian Freetrader takes it seriously.
According to his logic, a country that supplied
all its own needs could no longer support a
Government. Here is the logical conclusion of
the Freetrade theory that an increase in im-
portation is essential to the cure of a sick
Treasury. Incidentally it may be observed
that universal teetotalism—a great national
“ swearing off' it ’’—would rob an Exchequer of
visible means of support, whereas a sudden
abnormal consumption of foreign rum would
prove a blessing. It is all very droll, this
Argus story, but there is a moral in it. No
Victorian journal can be a protector of Fat
Man, the Revenue and the Farmer at the same
time. One of the three, at least, must be
abandoned. Seeing that Fat Man and the
Revenue are mixed up together in a mass of
Civil Service jobbery, why not dispense with
Revenue and kill the Government in preference
to the farmer? Heaven knows that Victoria
has much cause to curse its Government for
having been alive these last ten years.
“ Crushing the Liberal Party.”
Melbourne Age protests against the idea of
Speight, a ruined man, being able to raise
sufficient cash to support a libel-action against
the person who ruined him. In effect, the Age
says that Speight ought to have been kept out
of court by David Syme’s legal expenditure !
It was a disgraceful fact that he “ could ven-
ture to enter upon a transaction involving so
many immediate and contingent liabilities,”
because it proved, said the indignant Age, the
existence of “ a conspiracy to crush the Liberal
party.” What a Liberal line of argument!
The scandal of Speight’s action against the
Age really lay in its long postponement
whilst the mighty journal was fossicking
for means of dragging out the trial. The
cost of law is a sin against both parties,
under all circumstances, but a defendant who
encourages the law in its solemn swindle
by loading his defence with a mass of valueless
evidence with no visible result save waste of
time, has jar less cause for complaint than the
other fellow who strives to come to the point.
Syme’s case had crawled along for several
weeks before it showed a disposition to fight
Speight in real earnest, hence the general
belief that defendant’s counsel was manoeuvring
for a satisfactory compromise.
The Dirty Saints.
The latest news of that much - advertised
modern saint, W. T. Stead —the weird crank
who writes in Borderland concerning spooks
and goblins and the occult power of “ clair-
smellance ” —is that he has been preaching at
Chicago, where his manure-heap figures" of
speech have so aroused the city that he has
received a general notice to quit. Stead, in
the course of a deliverance at a ladies’ meeting,
told his auditors that they were “in many
respects worse than prostitutes hence the
trouble. The statement, in a broad sense,
was probably true, but the innate dirtiness of the
saint stands out conspicuously none the less.
It is a notable fact that most of the thrilling
saints of the day are more or less beastly—the
majority being more beastly. Poor old General
Booth, who has of late almost vanished out
of public remembrance, was eclipsed by the
meteoric rise of “Maiden Tribute” Stead, prin-
cipally through the want of judicious indecency.
Booth was always ignorant, hysterical and
foolish, and his hook - nose was suggestive
of the Hebrew money-lender, or the peadler of
Yankee “ notions ” rather than of the evan-
gelist, but even at his worst the gaunt old man
was never disgusting in his language, and when
another saint arose who was disgusting Booth’s
mana left him. Next to the Pope, Stead is pro-
bably thegreatest povverin the allegedly religious
world to-day. Most of the smaller stars in the
religious firmament seem to be built more or
less after his pattern. Varley, the glorified
meat-man, who had so great a vogue in Aus-
tralia, prospered chiefly through lecturing “ to
men only,” and being generally unfit for publi-
cation. That hoarse old songstress, Antoin-
ette Sterling, caused a furore, principally
because, no matter what subject she professed
to deal with, she presently drifted off to the
old, old story about the “ age of consent ”
and stayed there so long as her voice held out.
Antoinette’s popularity was merely another
evidence of the workings of the great law of
demand and supply. The clean saint may have
a short period of popularity, but there is no
permanent demand for him. It is only the
dirty saint that comes to stay, and the length
of time that he stays depends mostly on the
extent of his dirtiness. Are “ piety ” and
pruriency only different phases of the same
emotion ?
S.A. Advertiser publishes another letter from
“New Australia,” containing much joyful in-
formation about oranges and shady groves and
green verdure, and similar glories. An extract:
I only wish the poor old Bulletin critics could see us
now. They would then recognise the absurdity on their
prophesies anent_ the fate that awaited us in this country
of man-eating spiders and fierce mosquitoes.
The Bulletin here has to remind this excited
New Australian that the spider narratives re-
ferred not to Paraguay, but to the Argentine
plains, where the settlement was positively
located in the first instance, so the enthusiast
is out, as usual. Meanwhile, the New Australian
story is growing rather wearisome, by reason
of the columns of wild explanation in the daily
papers concerning the quarrels of the argonauts,
and the insinuations of tampering with her
correspondence levelled at the chief-pioneers,
by Mrs. Westwood, wife of the principal
seceder. This lady seems inclined to charge
them with intercepting letters which are not
sufficiently enthusiastic, and with a general
tendency to suppress communications which
are not bursting with fervour about oranges
and flowers and the like. Also, as the argo-
nauts are still living on the capital they brought
with them from Australia, and haven’t begun
to make a living under their new conditions,
the whooping is rather premature. The
latest news is that the next batch of argonauts
only take out one piano—the first lot, being
more enthusiastic, had two ; and that the cap-
tain of the Royal Tar has thrown up his post
owing to the insubordination of his crew of New
Australians.
One Pastor Savage, of Melbourne, has lately
been spreading himself in the papers upon the
threadbare subject of “godless education” in
Victoria. According to this good, but violent,
man, the population of Victoria, during the ten
years between 1881 and 1891, has increased 32
per cent, ; the total arrests have increased 46
per cent. ; illegitimate births have increased 49
per cent. ; and so on. And then comes the
overwhelming fact that the Sunday school atten-
dance has increased 50 per cent, in the same
period—from which it would seem that educa-
tion is becoming more gmlly instead of more
godless, and crime keeps growing as Sunday-
schools multiply. Further, the preacher goes
on to show that while crime is increasing
horribly in Victoria, in New South
Wales, which is almost equally “god-
less,” it is decreasing considerably. He
doesn’t attempt to account for this cir-
cumstance in any way. Next Statistician
Hayter rises to demonstrate that the good man’s
statistics are garbled and his conclusions unre-
liable. Victoria, he points out, contains a
vastly larger proportion of adults and a smaller
proportion of children than it did ten years
ago ; crime is almost exclusively committed by
adults; and the number of criminals, in propor-
tion to the adult population, has decreased
largely and is continuously decreasing. After
that the correspondence closes with another
outbreak from Parson Savage, in which he
dumps down his old assertion that crime arises
from “ godless education,” and leaves it at that.
Meanwhile the godly men of Melbourne con-
tinue to go to gaol in steady procession for
deficiency in the cash as heretofore.
Dear Bulletin, — I suppose what I’m going to
say here is a stale truism, but the fact
that it is known and glossed over makes it all
the more aggravating, and emphasises the all-
round rottenness of the New South Wales land-
laws. Not only is the “poor man without
capital ” hampered in a variety of ways in
settling on the land, but hundreds and thou-
sands of acres (to which they have no right) are
fenced in by squatters, semi-squatters and
managers of “bank” stations, and this fact,
though, of course, well-known to the neigh-
bouring landgrabbers, is unnoticed, because they
are in the same s-wim themselves. It would be
interesting to have laid before Parliament a
correct return, compiled by reliable authority—
most of those whose duty it is to look after such
matters are “ hand in glove ” with the pirates,
and therefore unreliable—showing the different
sections of Government land and their areas
fenced in and used by private parties with no
shadow of right. Take a strip of country —
say between Forbes and Bathurst—hardly a
Reserve or Common but is so enclosed, and the
traveller is driven off with threats and his
animals impounded. The brazen impudence
of some of these squatter kings is amazing, and
they know very well the trouble and incon-
venience it is to most, if not all, travellers to
go to law with them. Then they mostly have
traps —a chain or two of their legitimate pro-
perty unfenced in order to entrap your animals.
A man scarcely dare travel these days unless
he has a heap of maps delineating every district
he happens to pass through. To follow the
evil and curse in all its ramifications would
entail too long a letter—but I may add that, often
after years of usurpation, the squatters so pull
the strings as to obtain the laud altogether by
misrepresenting that it is unused and lying
waste.—Yours, Bushman.
No statement as to tjie probability of York and
Duchess visiting Australia next year is worth
the newspaper it is printed on. People who
fabricate these yarns forget, or do not choose
to remember, the awful importance which
royalty attaches to babies in the “ direct line.’'
Presuming that May’s first infant prove to be a
girl, she will not come to Australia for fear of
endangering the prospects of a boy in posse.
And if, on the other hand, the coming stranger
be an heir, she will neither bring its august
little person to Australia nor leave it in Eng-
land whilst she fools around the Antipodes.
Nor, if the baby—boy or girl—died in infancy,
would she make the journey, for a reason afore-
mentioned. In short, merciful Providence has
ordained that no visit from George and May
shall occur, and those alleged Royal Family
consultations on the subject are the merest
journalistic moonshine.
J. V. Chataway, Queensland M.L.A., and
black-labour apologist, damns his own side
deliberately thus
The statement that white men cannot work in the
cane-fields anywhere south of Townsville is obviously
untrue. White men can work in fair comfort, except
during three or four months of the year. In proof of this
I may say that besides whites cutting several thousand
tons of cane this year, white famers grew, cut, and
delivered at the mills at least 15,000 tons of cane which
were never touched by a coloured labourer.
Nine-tenths of the Q. sugar-crop are grown
south of Townsville, so that nine-tenths ©f
the Kanakas in Queensland may be dispensed
with at a blow. Chatawav goes on to allege
that the industry cannot pay white men’s
wages—in defiance of the fact that the white
farmers’ co-operative mills at Mackay last
season £12,000 to £14,000 profit, or £2
10s. per ton of cane, after paying 14s. per ton
to the growers. The truth is that the only
justification for employing the Kanaka is greed
—simple greed.

Alhambra Variety Cos.
“Girofld-Girofla,” an opera of no particular
place or date, but with a vague location on the
sea board of Bohemia, or thereabouts, has replaced
the “Royal Middy ” at Sydney Opera House.
The change is for the better ; there is more go in
the new production, and the chief objection to be
raised is the feeble efforts at localisation which
the co. makes as it goes along, and other un-
authorised interferences with the text. The opera
has that faint, far-off odour of medisevalism
which all the pre-Gilbert opera writers seemed to
consider indispensable. The artificial moss of the
dead centuries is upon it in spots, and the old-
time pirate with the shockingly-bashed hat is
introduced to lend an air of picturesqueness to
the story, likewise the brown-legged Moorish
marauder with the astonishingly short petticoat
and the saddle-coloured visage. And there is
much placid, blue and beautifully-painted Medi-
terranean in the far distance, with the lateen-sail
of old Dragut, or some equally outrageous
Moresco on the horizon, “ Girofle-Girofla ”is a
lively opera while it keeps going, and there are
cool and restful spots in between, when the pano-
rama of the Mediterranean feels like an iced
drink in a dry land amid a year of drought.

Miss Colbourne-Baber, as the twin sisters,
has improved. Her singing voice is as good as it
was, and her speaking voice is less uncertain than
la it week. Also a certain rustiness begotten of a
r vther prolonged absence from the operatic stage is
fading away; her hinges have been oiled, so to
speak, and she plays her part with an obvious and
e.icouraging sense of humour. Edward Farley
p ay s Mourzouk in an unrecognisable voice, and wi th
b'.oodthirsty vigour and an evident yearning for
human flesh; and Mr. Harry Craig presents a
wrinkled front to the character of Bolero, and
makes an unusual success out of that doddering
potentate. Further, the usually statuesque
Walshe, who generally seems as if he would like
to go out into the wilderness and think seriously
about his own extreme height, is less statuesque
than usual, and, in addition to being in excellent
voice, he throws himself about affably with the
abandon of a very large man who has cast off all
restraint. The rest of the co., barring one voice
which seems to come from the pirate in the
affrighted hat, is well up to the mark, and the
well-drilled chorus goes like one tight, or one
pair of tights, whichever the reader pleases.

“ Charley’s Aunt ” has taken deep root at
Syd iey Criterion, and the house is crammed
nightly. Still, a longer acquaintance with this
excellent and most unprecedentedly profitable
comedy reveals sundry serious defects. Probably
the worst of these is the long and agonising confab
between the Brazilian aunt and her adopted
daughter—a massive dialogue done in accents of
wailing, in the course of which they grievously
explain various things that the audience would
much sooner take for granted. This dialogue is
bad enough when it is heard for the first time ;
but the second time it is like a large presentation
of pig-lead. Also, the specially-imported mem-
bers of the com pany are dismally commonplace.
Two of them are understood to have been im-
ported because of their special knowledge of the
manners, dialect, and general tone of the Hinglish
student, but they merely succeed in demonstrating
that the Hinglish student is only a harmless dupli-
cate of anybody else.
*« * *
Increased familiarity with the book of
“Beauty and the Beast,” at Sydney Lyceum,
confirms The Bulletin in its opinion that the
said book was written by the Beast. As the
cheerless publication, however, has practically
nothing to do with the show, this is a matter of
no importance. “ Beauty ” travels on its loose
gorgeousness, its utter irrespectiveness of expense,
its wild regardlessness of consequences and
Bartho. Every time this pantomime is witnessed
its lavishness strikes the observer afresh. Even
Nellie Stewart is only an incident in the
show, and Florence Young is another, and Bartho
herself doesn’t stand out so conspicuously as she
did. The effect of the whole dazzle is a general
feeling that one has been hit on the head with the
Koh-i-noor, and lias then gone out and taken an
oyster supper with a bird of Paradise. The bur-
den of “Beauty’s” magnificence grows weari-
some after a while, and when the auditor gets
away he wants to walk several times round a
church so as to bring himself back into a plain
and serious state of mind.
«* « «
George Rignold’s pantomime of “Jack,
the Giant-Killer,” continues to do well at Sydney
Her Majesty’s, and the principal giant, who might
let himrelf out as a very reasonable church-spire,
looks as healthy as heretofore. At this show also
the utter and hopeless flabbergasted ness of the
book has been surmounted by excellent scenic
effects and the combined efforts of a good com-
Rany, aided by the fact that the lay of Marietta
Fash is not so hoarse as it was, that lady’s cold
having been judiciously treated with eucalyptus
rubbed into the chest. Therefore, The Bulletin
will merely remark that the said book ought to be
cast down a Cimmerian gulf into Erebus, and
there trodden underfoot by horned demons for
evermore.
** * *
Walton and Gourlay’s pantomime still
draws good houses at Sydney Royal, and the
funny man, who is a weak spot elsewhere, comes
out strong and well in the “ Babes.” George
Walton’s bold buccaneer is a comic sailor who
doesn’t need billing to that effect, and if all Babes
knew as much as Johnny Gourlay and the lemon-
ade bottle-supported Miss Harte, the occupation
of Mrs. Makin would not have been possible. A
pleasant thing about the “ Babes ” is the absence
of the girl in a yard of gauze and a wand, who
sings a knock-knee’d ballad about where the roses
bloom in the starlight from the footlights to hide
the muffled roar of the scene-shifters.
«* » *
The “Crystal Maze,” at the Strand,
Sydney, n >w supplies its visitors with ice-creams
and refreshments gratis. The Maze is that curious
place of mirrors where the visitor gets lost among
innumerable reflections and can’t discover his way
out again, and the real ice-cream will be difficult
to find owing to the mirage of a thousand unreal
ioe-creams iu the glass.
Ihe magnificence of the pantomime at
Melb. Princess’s grows rather oppressive. Some-
how it smacks of a meeting between King
Salomon in his dotage, and the Queen of Sheba
when she had the ’ump. Cold splendour has
settled on the liver of the great production, and
its health is getting trampled down by processions.
Some people are even saying that Lonnen’s panto-
mime of last year was preferable to this, but The
Bulletin declines lo endorse any such extreme
statement. Nothing can be gloomier than the
haunting memory of Lonnen’s smile. Don Juan
de Somebody, whose castle is in Spain, now places
a chair upon the slack-wire and balances himself
upon the chair with Japanese precision, yet,
unless this skilled performer consents to in-
troduce a shocking accident by way of novelty,
he will soon be reckoned dear at JB70 a
week. Boy Blue and Red Riding Hood pall
upon the public taste, whilst Simple Simon
is enlarging into a theme for general exe-
cration. The intentions of Simon are strictly
honourable, of course, but his humour taxes the
resources of the Australian language to their
utmost. He is as painful as a whitlow on one’s
wounded spirit. Last week this gruesome mummer
introduced a larger stuffed dog than his previous
stuffed dog, with the remark that it had become a
Freetrader after its eyes were opened. For-
tunately this musty wheeze will assuredly be
withdrawn to oblige the Age, which is a
Protectionist journal. For the Age’s iron
hand has already made one little grab at
the managerial throat of W. and M., even
as, three years ago, it tried to choke George
Coppin. He chokes loudest who chokes last.
Topical allusions likely to distress the Age are
strictly prohibited, ’tis said. For example, there
is a song with the tender refrain of “It takes a
girl to do it every time.” Suppose the warbler
were to try a new verse after this style :
Our Jones has just departed from a weary world of woe ;
He will jerk the fatal lever nevermore ;
Though he did a lot of damage to his brothers here below
He drew ihe line at killing Mrs. Knorr.
A dozen trousered Deernings he could drop without a sob,
And see their bodies laid in prison lime ;
O, to make a common hangman get disgusted with his
job—
It takes a girl to do it every time.
Would that verse reflect Age sentiments concern-
ing capital punishment for women ? No.
* * * *
Dan Barry’s drama of bygone days is
holding on for a third week at the Alexandra.
They did droll things in the early infancy of this
young country. Bushrangers had a way of pur-
loining poor selectors in order that their wives
might raise the necessary ransom from a long,
lean squatter on terms which a sixpenny periodical
would blush to mention. However, seeing that
the ladies always escaped with their honours thick
upon them, leaving the bloated capitalist to come
to a bad end, it is a matter for regret that
Providence can’t push back the universe to the
days of auld lang syne. Barry’s capable company
has come to stay, seemingly. Next Saturday
(13th) is to dawn upon the production of a new
and exciting drama by Scudamore, a famous
English dealer in rampageous works of art.
* * * *
Maggie Moore, with the Melb. Royal
panto, in tow, is steaming along at high pressure.
Garnet Walch built this seaworthy craft—mean-
ing the panto., not M.M.—and every here and
there he hammered in a brass-headed joke, carry-
ing a point at its latter end. “ Sinbad ” has also
solid pretensions to amuse children, which is no
small matter as things go. When a boy laughs,
his honoured parent can usually laugh with him,
whereas the old man may chuckle for ten minutes
on his own account without extracting the least
mirth from the members of his family. A fine,
substantial fairy Queen is a useful item at the
Royal. This lady has a cheerful, worldly way
with her, indicating that her mother was a prac-
tical fairy, who married a mortal. Tom Queen’s
acrobatic business has been well thought out,
and the Permans seem specially pleasing in
that they are known to comprise a pair of
genuine brothers and a real pretty sister. There is
a certain fresh and breezy innocence about a Bella
dancing under these conditions, a frank and free
clasping of hands and waists. Now, the ordinary
stage brother is too evidently married to his
“ sister.” Their pretence at being on affectionate
terms is ghastly in the extreme, Leumane is not
very breezy, but his old, reliable top-note can find
its way to the roof, a portion of the building which
is nightly lifted off by Grist’s panorama and
Brunton’s transformation-scene. The transfor-
mation in question dates back to last Xmas, but
it shines again with undiminished lustre.
* * * *
J. 0. Williamson sailed for London on
Tuesday of this week in quest of general novelties
—and another Gaiety Company, perhaps. Thus
far he has dissembled this deadly purpose.
At Poverty Point.
During last Melb. floods showman Kreitmeyer lived
down Richmond way. Ue had a number of effigies in
the back-shed; these were all washed down the Yarra,
and the spectacle of a fat gentleman who dived in only to
rescue a golden-haired wax Cleopatra much amused the
sorrowing neighbourhood. He simply stamped the
object of his solicitude into pulp—and then had to pay
for his heroism.
Since the retirement of Dr. Neild there has been in
Melbourne no essentially dramatic critic who is permitted
to be a specialist at the work. Reporting the meeting of
shareholders of the Jupiter Pluvius Building Society in
the day, and criticising the new play at the Prin jess’s the
same night is part of the fitness of things. Often the
reporters don’t go near the theatre at all. In a recent
notice on somebody’s concert the “ finished playing of
Monsieur Tremolo on the oboe ” was complimentarily
referred to in about half-a-dozen lines. But that cannot
be finished which was not beeun, and it so happened that
Monsieur Tremolo, being ill, his number was cut out
entirely. In one of the dailies “A Bad Penny” was
alluded to as “ an amusing little farce.”
Two tiny twin sisters are the best joke in Melb. Royal
panto. They have two little companion parts to play
and each toddler tries to take the shine out of the other.

Capt. Humphreys, the ex-lion-tamer, or rather what
remains of that much-chewed veteran, has now taken to
safer business, and is agent and lecturer for a preparation
called “Chloe Oil," warranted to cure rheumatism,
sciatica, lumbago, sprains, bruises, chest complaints,
neuralgia, sores, chilblains, burns and bites of insects.
Anyone who goes up or down Sydney Harbour is bound
to hear of Chloe Oil, for its painted “ad." disfigures
nearly every beauty spot from the Heads to the top of
Lane Cove.

A lot of Melb. girls have taken to learning Italian, the
mellifluous language of the fascinating Giraud and his
compeers. Giraud’s English didn’t get further than

man of the troupe. He looked a dapper little soul in

Mr. and Mrs. Q. B. W. Lewis contemplate a tour in
South Africa. Neither of them is quite young enough to
make a big success in the new Tom Tiddler’s ground.
They rely in some degree upon the good offices of Lady
Loch, but vice-royalty counts for little amongst the
Afrikanders, especially just now when their backs are
up with Downing-street about the Matabele business.
Courtneidge, the actor, was the innocent means of
getting an unanimous theatre verdict in the great rail-
way libel-case. The bare news of £lOO damages for
Speight was telephoned to Melb, Princess’. By the time
it reached the stage, where the pantomime was proceed-
ing, the £lOO had increased seventyfold. When Court-
neidge brought out a wheeze to the effect that Speight
would draw £7OOO from David Syme, the audience
applauded like one great clap of thunder. There was but
a single dissentient, who complained in a piping treble
that the amount wasn’t half enough. About 99 per cent,
of the house cheered vigorously. Among the one
per cent, that didn’t jubilate was Purves, Q. 0.,
and he rushed from the dress-circle, vowing that
Courtneidge had plotted to bring contumely upon
a noble newspaper. For a time it was understood
that Purves would commit the comedian for contempt of
court, or something. Subsequently they liquored up
together and Courtneidge announced, “by special desire,”
that Speight’s verdict was for £lOO and not £7OOO.
Whereat the audience murmured “ O-o-oh ” in accents of
heartfelt sorrow.

A leading London theatrical agent affirms that 70 per
cent, of English actors and actresses are out of work.
He counts 97 “juvenile ladies” disengaged out of 236,
and 215 “second juveniles " out of 315.

Linger longer, Lucy—linger longer, Loo

Linger longer, longer linger, linger longer, Loo.
One of Arthur Roberts’mild jokelets in “Don Juan"
consists in wearing a bathing costume and floundering
about on the stage after the manner of a stout, elderly
gentleman in shallow water, with a bathing song accom-
paniment. When this item came on the first night, critic
Archer rose and sternly departed the premises.
The item in the “ Don Juan " burlesque which led to
the intervention of the Ottoman Ambassador was the
violent and opprobrious kicking of the Sultan of Turkey
all over the stage. The Bulletin’s old friend, Oarington,
as the chief official censor of plays, had passed the offen-
sive item, which is another serious reflection on Cham-
pagne Smith’s intelligence, or whatever he calls it.
Writing of an elderly actress, a critic once said: “Her
beautiful diamond necklace reminds one of a lantern on a
wreck.”
A well-known Australian actor was one night playing
an old man part, and in the interval he took off his wig
to cool his parched head and forgot all about it. When
the curtain rose on the second act he burst in without it.
The other man, however, was equal to the occasion and
said: “It is not you 1 want, but your father.” Then
the actor bolted off and presently re-entered with his
wig. “Warned by my son,” he said, gravely, “ I have
hastened hither.”

846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney,
January 10, 1804.
My Dear Moorabinda—
Sydney D.T.’s yard-and-a-half of stuff on the
subject of His Excellency Sir Robert Duff, meant
to pacify the enraged Macdougals, and so on,
wouldn’t deceive an infant. Regarded as an in-
spired defence, it is simply abject in its clumsi-
ness.
One thing is clearly shown by all this awkward-
ness—Sir Robert Duff is not an airy diplomat
who does what he likes and yet keeps everybody
in a simmer of loyalty. Imagine with what facile
humour, what graceful persiflage, such a man as
Lord Rosebery—the Governor who might have
been (not that there was ever much chance of his
coming)—would have disposed of the clumsy
“scene” created by those tactless Scotchmen!
Gov. Duff's impolitic apologetic speech, at the
Commercial Travellers’ dinner, was altogether in
the wrong vein. He “ understood that even a
Governor was allowed to have his Xmas holidays
to himself.” But did he really imagine that the
Scotchmen would give a whiskyless holiday to a
“ brither,” or think to escape them on New Year’s
Day?
If what one viceroy does the next must do, I
really think that (judging from the strained
relations between N.S.W. Govmentouse and the
local Hielandmen, on account of the Governor
not being present —for the first time in 25 years—
at their annual festival) the first care of a
Governor should be as to how his acts will affect his
successor. An itinerant Governor should curb
his wandering propensities lest, when a lazier
potentate comes along, the men of Dog Elat
should be heard hooting in the distance because
His Ex. won’t journey 6000 miles to open a
pumpkin-show.
The satrap who loves to be popular and is
pleased to pumphandle the multitude at frequent
intervals, in order to be cheered on his departure,
might pause and reflect what sort of a row will
occur if the next man happens to resent the claw-
ing process? And if Governors won’t consider
one another there should be framed reg’lations
hung up in every Govmentouse for the guidance
and protection of the various vice-regalities. At
present a Governor governs according to his idio-
syncrasy, and every man has an uphill fight to
make his particular method of- civilising the
savages popular.
Gov. Duff’s foiled admirers, who are only fair-
weather friends, seemingly, should remember,
when they are airing their soreness, that His Ex.
came here as an admittedly professional Governor
and that he attends all official shivoos at his own
pleasure entirely. To what extent a Governor
may exclude himself, if he choose, was sternly
shown by Lord Augustus Loftus, who calmly
turned Sydney’s social starting-point into a
poultry-yard and stoically stayed out his term of
office, despite the outcries of the baffled female in
a yellow bonnet who sought to be a sister to Lady
The men in kilts should remember
that His Ex. can, if he so pleases, take to grow-
ing cabbages in the’ Inner Domain and retire to
undisturbed contemplation of the speechless slug.
Damsels who adopt fiercely-public callings must
expect a few shocks now and then, but it is doubt-
ful if anybody has ever got such a staggerer in the
very blush of her wild career as did a certain Aus-
tralian society young lady who is encouraging her
dramatic genius by donning the fleshings of a
figurante. The thing occurred at a dress rehear-
sal, The society girl, unbroken to the compass-
like straddlings of Flossie, refused to widen her
pedestal at the stage-manager’s bidding, upon
which that case-hardened individual remarked—
well, I’d really like to say just what he said as a
warning to young ladies who regard the stage as
their excelsior; but I won’t, because I cannot
here talk behind my fan.
But dissuading a maiden from her chosen Fate
is always an ungrateful undertaking. The girl
who is “ called ” to be a Sister Dora, with dreams
of spooning jelly all over the nice moustache of
some grateful patient who wears navy serge and
brass buttons when “in condition,” never sees
that her genius lies in quite a different direction
until she is called upon to wash a dead Chinese
hawker. And all the full-flavoured stage-mana-
gerials in creation would not. scare one dogged
girl out of the notion that she is the makings of
the only Juliet.
Dr. Youl, the Melbourne Coroner, soared far
above red-tape in his crisp contempt for the chief
cause of the recent sensational double-drowning at
St. Kilda. A girl who had loved Mr. Birtles
too well jumped off the pier, whereupon Mr. B.
calmly looked round for a life buoy, while a gal-
lant stranger named William Satchell (leaving his
own sweetheart for ever) dived in to save the girl,
and was drowned in the attempt. At the double-
inquest Birtles told the plain, ugly truth, to the
expressed contempt of the Coroner, who, like all
legal officials, expects the most unblushing perjury
where a woman is concerned, although it is his

official duty to thresh out the truth, the whole
truth, and nothing but the truth—or why make
people kiss the book about it?
But the fact is that Truth isn’t Justice, nor is
Justice Truth—they are two separate figures.
Their allegories are two distinct women, and you
may palliate one or the other, not necessarily
both. All that a Court wants to happen is Jus-
tice, and unpleasant facts don’t always help
along the consummation. That, I presume, is
what Dr. Youl means to convey.
I have often thought that the lies (the best
specimens occur every day in the divorce-courts)
men brazenly tell when the cat is out of the bag,
are a good cloak that chivalry lends to the man
whose stage-heroic magnanimity shields himself
as well ks the woman. I know lam pleading an
unpopular and unheroic, and, if you like, essen-
tially contemptible cause, but, all the same, we
ought not to be too severe on Birtles. The same-
want of nerve which stopped him from jumping
into the water and saving the wreck he was
responsible for, probably induced him to tell
astonishing and unprecedented truths when he
attended the inquest. Lies and nerve are often
convertible terms —a nervous person states bald
and hideous facts because he hasn’t the cool
assurance to carry out a pleasant and feasible
fiction.
The minute received from Attorney-General
Heydon by the Newcastle (N.S.W.) Council to
the effect that “the bye-laws recently framed
authorising the bathing on the sea-beach by per-
sons of either sex were ultra vires ,” may be good
law, but all the same it is most characteristic of
the present regime ! The new Attorney-General
is a most austere gentleman who seems always on
the verge of tying a rope round his waist and
bolting into a friary away from this wicked world.
Not even the wildest flight of fancy could picture
any woman taking an especial delight in diving
after cockles with the Honorable Mr. Heydon.
While even one solitary male professor of
Natation exists you can’t say there is no such
thing as dual bathing; and by forbidding the
general mingling, I take it that you censure every
woman who is taught how to float by Professor
Jellyfish? Which somehow reminds me of an
over-true incident that happened recently at a
ladies’ bath in a fashionable Sydney Harbour
suburb. The wife of a local resident had been
taught a double-somersault by her husband, and
during the bath she requested the company —in-
cluding the professor—to act as audience while
she turned a flip-flap into the water; so they all
ranged round the brink. “ Mind you watch me
carefully,” said the heroine, and then over she
went —but even bathing-suits wear out, and —but
let me draw the metaphorical veil which was so
much needed actually. The bathing-man simply
fled, while«the women on the edge felt that death
would have been sweeter.
It isn’t often that two bad authors get
grievously wounded in their tenderest feelings
almost in the same hour, but that is what happened
on opening-night to the merciless composers of a
couple of Baratarian pantomimes. The lessee of
one house sat behind the author of the rival show
in company with an artist. “Great Caesar! it’s
as bad as our man’s book,” quoth the lessee,
unconscious that the criminal was wriggling in
front.
Afterwards, at aspirituous rendezvous, the artist
met the author of the other pantomime, who was
personally unknown to him. “You should have
heard how Bratus Snap gave it to the author of
‘Blue Beans,’” he said, confidentially. “There
he was, sitting close by, and Snap said, * Great
Caesar! it’s as bad as our man’s book.’” How-
ever, the worse the poet the thicker the hide.
Probably he only smiled pityingly, and remarked
that his wit was a littlo too subtle for some
people.
revelry, I am told, led to a unique
spectacle in a N.S.W. country town. The local
parson had an emu which was greatly addicted to
strong drink, so whenever the local youths felt
inclined for a little diversion they made the bob-
tail fowl as irresponsible as the legendary Chloe.
On Xmas eve a bright thought occurred to the
bird’s admirers, and they stole silently up to the
parsonage paddock, while the priest was at tea,
and intoxicated the willing emu as usual. Then
they put a pair of snowy-white, tucked-and-frilled
garments on the emu’s staggering legs, a gathered
shirt sat well on the jutting wings and tail, and
when the dress-hat had been tied on to its dodder-
ing head, the toilet was complete. Then they
turned it loose into the township in the glow of
the setting sun.
Tufuhan, Tonga’s brand-new 17-years-old King,
is said to be a handsome youth, rapidly spreading
into obesity. On his arrival from Fiji the match-
making crones of his kingdom set about finding a
bride tor him, settling on a girl of 11 and another
of 12 as the Queen. But Tufuhau had learnt
more than English in Fiji, so he calmly dismissed
the candidates and expressed hia intention of re-
maining a bachelor—certainly a unique state in
which to find a king. The unmarried potentate
doesn’t occur half often enough in the Court cir-
culars. Any colour-blind itinerant spinster who
yearns after a kindred soul might bear this royal
bachelor in mind when setting out on her travels.

The dreadfully sudden death of Mr, George
Rowley, secretary of the Rosehill and Kensington
Race Clubs, who was killed in a buggy accident
on New Year’s Eve, will not be soon forgotten,
for he will be greatly missed. In accordance with
a wish expressed in his will there were no mourn-
ing coaches and no hearse, and the coffin was
carried to the grave on the shoulders of eight
friends.
That is, after all, a true test of friendship. How
many of us are sure that we have eight true
friends who would perform such a sad duty in a
tender spirit—and how many of ns are true
enough friends ourselves to merit such a final
mark of love? However, some people have a
swarm of acquaintances in the guise of friends
who would be rather glad of the chance to carry
them to the grave, and plant them as deep as pos-
sible.
“ Charley’s Aunt ” has galvanized all the old
B. & B. enthusiasm into life at Sydney Criterion.
In the curtain-lifter, “A Bad Penny,” there is
not much dressing, and in one instance not dress-
ing enough. There is a certain unwritten law re
the perforations of the clothes of the stage dead-
beat, whom the “unities” never allow to be ven-
tilated above the knee-cap, however wildly moths
may behave in nature.
The only Frank Thornton wore a rich black
satin gown as the Aunt, but the dressmaker takes
a welcome back seat in the comedy, which has
more than rags to run on. The ohirrupy Miss
Dot Frederics (Mrs. Arnold) wore a very dreamy
confection in the faint colours of French candy in
the last act, and Miss Agnes Knight, in a grey
and white gown, looked like an impressionist idea
of a seagull.
“Charley’s Aunt” is sprinkled with jaunty
speeches embodying many sophistries. For in-
stance, “a buttonholer takes ten years off a man’s
life,” is only true up to a certain point. For my
part, whenever I see an overblown male with a
boutonniere I always look closer to see what par-
ticular ravage of time it’s a cloak for. If the
decoration is gaudy scarlet, it is generally sported
to subdue the warm nose-tints, and if it is large
and yellow it’s always worn to counteract facial
evidence to the effect that the liver moves in a
mysterious way its wonders to perform ; while an
elderly man with a white flower always strikes one
as anticipating the floralities of his own funeral.
An old man with a flower in his coat is almost as
hideous as an old woman with rouged lips.
Another thing that ruffled me in Brandon
Thomas’s play was the real aunt’s silly “good
story ” when the men were “off.” I confess my
flesh crept a little just there, because it is an
exploded notion that women’s stories are point-
less things. For, though the average man has
merely to recapitulate some of his personal experi-
ences to amuse the club, while, for obvious
reasons, a woman daren’t make use of any spicy
personal memoirs of her own to liven up the tea-
table, still there are she-stories in the third person
that end just a little more definitely than Donna
Lucia’s. Men judge a woman’s genius for point-
finding by the studied imbecilities that she
designedly treats them to; but clever women
don’t judge a man’s ability as a raconteur by what
they hear him talking about. At least, no mar-
ried woman does.

It is cabled that Maria Jones, a member of the
London Gaiety Co. which visited Australia last,
is dead. Maria weighed more than half-an-ounce,
and her funniest aspect was when she used to
fall in the garden-scene of “Faust up to Date ”
and shake the property trees to the roots. My
most vivid recollection of the lady is a vision of
her as Martha, walking into the wings amorously
clinging to the arm of Lonnen, dressed as the
Devil.
The extremely amusing pantomime,' “ Babes in
the Wood,” at Sydney Royal, “ isn’t too dressy but
just dressy enough,” in the words of George
Walton. I liked Miss Cecily {Staunton’s yellow
satin dress with clusters round the flounce of blue
cornflowers—cornflower velvet bodice and a
butterfly headdress of spangled lace and corn-
flowers. Miss Staunton wore an unusual mixture
of blue and brown in the last act, when Flora
Hastings was gorgeously arrayed in mulberry
satin with petticoat and half sleeves of lavender
silk brocaded in purple velvet flowers —Eliza-
bethan collar of white lace and stage diamonds
everywhere. The lively Ada Juneen looked
prettiest in a satin sailor-suit with pale-blue
tights, and the most gorgeous Queen in the
chronologically-correct procession was Elizabeth
in white satin embroidered in gold and pearls and
priceless jewels, seemingly. I didn’t like one
thing about the final transformation. That pale-
blue girl rising from a split bivalve was too fear-
fully suggestive of a figurante on a half-shell.

A general free fight has started between
the comic republic of Honduras and its neighbour,
Nicaragua. At present Nicaragua is uppermost.
The reason of the war is obscure, but there is no
doubt about the tooth-and-nail energy with which
the matter will be fought out on both sides.
The Central American may have nothing parti-
cular to fight for, and never quite knows what the
trouble is about, but he dies cheerfully on top of
his broken gun with his teeth fixed in the calf of
the enemy.
Dear Bulletin, —More traces of Leich-
hardt ! Numbers of half-castes have recently
been discovered in Port Darwin (N.T.), and in
Perth (W.A.), and at intermediate ports. Poor
Leichhardt and party must have put in much
time on the coast —they can be traced back fully
40 years by the ages of the half-castes, if the fore-
going reasoning be correct. In fact, Leichhardt
and party must have been here within the last 12
months, as we have half-castes a few weeks old.
Baby half-castes are called “ little Leichhardts ”
here in Port Darwin. —Boko.
