The North Eton sugar-mill, Mackay (Q.),
paid 13s. 6d. per ton for cane to white co-operating
farmers, and made a large profit; the Kanaka
mills everywhere else paid Bs. to 11s. 3d., and
made a gigantic profit.
Oswald Brierley, the English sea-scape
artist, is 80. He accompanied Ben Boyd, from
England to Twofold Bay, N.S.W., in the
Wanderer yacht, half-a-century ago. Again he
was with the Duke of Edinburgh in the Galatea.
It comes with a shock to see A. C. Swin-
burne among the ordinary lot of Brown and
Smith passengers to Australia, by the Oruba.
The great poet doesn’t look a bit like his reputa-
tion. But he is great —at least, pretty great.
Perhaps Justin M'Oarthy’s criticism is as apt as
any. Justin says that Swinburne shot his literary
bolt higher than any of his contemporaries, but
he shot only one bolt.
Dear Bulletin, —Here is a curious coin-
cidence : There were' only ten shearers at (say
Boulah Downs shed last year. Their names were
Wolseley, Lamb, Shepherd, Bales, Press, Shears,
Legge, Fogg, Bell, and Warte. We thought the
last four names were out of place, but Legge got
the run, Fogg cleared, Bell “ rung ” the shed, and
Warte turned out to be a “scab.” — Boiling
Billy.
The bebaubled E. T. Smith “ has declined
the overtures of both political parties ” to stand as
a candidate for the Commons in his native borough
of Wallsall; and also further declines to re-enter
South Australian politics. This is “ Moiety ”
Smith, who, when Mayor of Adelaide, declared
that the local fire-brigade fund should be thus
contributed —one moiety by Govt., one moiety by
the fire-insurance societies, and one moiety by the
Corporation ! Smith, from being a humble employe
of the Kent Town Brewery (Adelaide), became the
owner of that establishment. The mayoral con-
test between him and the pious John Colton, in
the year in which “ the princes ” were coming in
H.M.S. Bacchante, is historic, and cost Smith a
few thousands. The Adelaide press then pro-
phesied that Mayor Smith, who was chiefly
remarkable for a long beard and longer generosity,
would be made a Bart.
Miss Jessie Ackerman, the she-evangelist
from Boston, who lately infested Australia, has
got “ Hum ” after pervading this tired sphere for
five years. She travelled 100,000 miles; visited
502 cities; held 1417 meetings; delivered 870
lectures and 447 addresses ; spoke on 41 vessels ;
visited 290 schools and 69 Bands of Hope ; preached
in 182 pulpits; initiated 647 Good Templers and
about 8000 other teetotallers ; visited 897 public-
houses, and remonstrated with the people drinking
therein; established about 250 lodges ; marched
at the head of processions in 52 different cities ;
received 3486 letters; wrote 5947 letters, 420 news-
paper articles, 220 letters to European papers ; and
published 60,000 tracts. Also, she travelled by
camel, horse, mule and elephant; steamers, skiffs,
sailing vessels and junks, stage, carriage, ekka,
tonga, jeninch, buffalo, bullock and camel carts,
garah, sedan chair, palanquin and wheelbarrow,
and went down in a diving-bell. Truly it is
an awful record of fast trotting and wasted loco-
motion and the wild skurryings of a jawful female
over five continents. There is no land so remote
that the flop of her loose shoes hasn’t been heard,
and her skirts haven’t raised a dust there, and
there is no known highway where she hasn’t fled
along as a horrid example of the Woman who
Won’t Keep Still.
So many “Australian” stories are just
now seeing the light that it is a real pleasure
to welcome a collection of tales by an author
who knows what he is writing about. “Steve
Brown’s Bunyip and other Stories” (Reming-
ton and Co., Limited), by J. A. Barry (“L.L.”),
is a book quite good enough to stand alone
without the introductory verses by Rudyard
Kipling. The Anglo-Indian story-teller has
written worse rhymes, certainly, but he has
also written ever so much better. Barry’s stories
are by sea and land, and, if there is a difference,
we incline to the sea tales. They are fully equal
in picturesque detail to anything of Clark
Russell’s, without the somewhat ostentatious
vulgarity which so often disfigures that author’s
works. Both in the land and sea tales there is,
what so few stories nowadays possess, the per-
vading feeling of locality ; the scenery, whether it
be in the township of Jillibegee, “frying in the
sun,” 650 miles north-west, or in the foc’sle of the
Sardanapalus, is not a mere word photograph but
a living picture. Moreover, Barry’s bushmen and
diggers are not cowboys or Californians masquerad-
ing in Australian dress, but such Australian types
as are to be found. We have not yet, it is true, had
time to develops any strikiijp national charac-
teristics, and herein the Australian author lies at
some disadvantage; but such books as “Steve
Brown’s Bunyip” will help to demolish the bas-
tard imitations of Bret Harte’s creations which
have been so long inflicted on the world as typical
Australian bushmen. The best and most finished
of the stories in this volume are the first and title
one, “Ex Sardanapalus,” “The Shanghai-ing of
Peter Barlow,” “ The Protection of the Sparrow-
hawk,” “Books at Barracaboo,” and a “Cape
Horn Christmas.” All the others are racy and
interesting, although the most ambitious one,
in point of length, appears to lack a connecting
link in the narrative. We are also in a position
to assure the reader that there are neither convicts
nor bushrangers in these stories. We quote the
best two verses out of Kipling’s introductory
poem ;
The irood wife’s sons come home again
Wi’ little in their hands
Rut the iear o’ men that ha’ dealt wi’ men
In the new and naked lands.
Rut the faith o’ men that ha’ proven men
Ry more than willing breath,
And the eyes o’ men that ha’ read wi’ men
In the open ho' ks o’ Death.

In a Melb. court a judge lately got quite
savage, because a workman witness said a married
man got less wages than a single one. “ How is
that, sir?” “Because the employer knows you
have got some sticks, and cannot leave.”
A daughter of Singer, the sewing-machine
man, married the Due Decazes. Someone who
saw her for the first time at a function exclaimed,
amazedly ;“ Is that a real Duchess?” “Yes,”
,was the reply, “but machine-made.”
Pat O’Neil, a Hokitika milkman, was on 6
morning carrying round his cans when his doB
stuck up a Chinese vendor of “whi’ bai.” Th e
Chow fled, leaving the two kerosene-tins behind
him. Pat set down his milk-pails and ran to the
rescue. Seizing his opportunity, a rival milkman
meanwhile transferred a couple of pints of white
bait from the Chinaman’s tins into Pat’s, whose
next customer wanted to know how on earth the
fish had got into the milk. Pat, blushing to the
roots of his hair, stammered apologetically
“Well, bad luck to me, ma’am, if you’d ever believe
a fwhite bait could swhim up that creek.”
Victoria has reason to complain of the
invalidism of its judges. Justice Hodges has
been somewhat impaired in re Speight, and, in
the past, Stawell, Stephen, Webb, Williams and
Kerferd have struggled, at considerable public
disadvantage, to get through the work, landing
not so much themselves as the publio in heavy
expense, whereas such as Higinbotham, Moles-
worth and Holrsyd have never cost the tax-
payer an extra twopence beyond their salaries.
At latest advices Madden is not well. Now,
in England, it is coming into vogue to ap-
point youngish judges; even a brilliant man of
35, with 10 years’ practice, is fit for a judge-
ship, instead of waiting till he has used himself
up at the bar, and the judgery is a mere haven,
like that of the fat, lazy, old cock-schnapper, who
lies in his own peculiar hole at the bottom of Port
Jackson —pensioned on periwinkles and things.
Dear Bulletin, —The swagman is really
the bushworker’s worst enemy, as he is a constant
menace to a man’s security of tenure of his billet,
and enables employers to lower wages because the
employes fear that their billets will only too
readily be snapped up at any wages offered. T
would suggest to the Australian workers the
following remedies for the swagger. (1) Force
your Government to put such a tax on land as
will make it impossible for anyone to hold country
without working it. We are doing it here. (2)
No more strikes. A strike is a bad weapon that
shoots harder behind than it does in front. Votes
are the things, (3) Keep a cheque when you get
one. Close the swagger’s port of departure— the
lambing-down shanty. The man who habitually
knocks down his cheque is a slave of the worst
kind, and other workers have to compete with his
slave-labour. As John Burns says;—“At the
bottom of all labour troubles is the liquor
trouble.” —Yours, &c., Waipoie, Gisborne, M.L.,
Deo. 10, 1893. [John Burns’ statement is
specious, but not sound. The liquor trouble is
often at the top of labour troubles. Poverty
causes more drink than, or at least as much as,
drink causes poverty. Drunkenness is largely a
medical and hygienic question—a question too
vast to he dealt with in an epistolary footnote.—
Ed, B ]
A Vision of Youth.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
A horseman on a hill-top green
Drew rein, and wound his horn ;
So bright he looked he might have been
The Herald of the Morn.
His steed was of the sovran strain
In Fancy’s meadows bred—
And Pride was in his tossing mane,
And Triumph in his tread.
The rider’s eyes like jewels glowed—
The World was in his hand—
As down the woodland way he rode
When Spring was in the land.
From golden hour to golden hour
For him the woodland sang,
And from the heart of ev’ry flow’r
A singing fairy sprang.
He rode along with rein so free,
And, as he rode, the Blue
Mysterious Bird of Fantasy
Ever before him flew.
He rode by castle and by cot,
Through all the greenland gay ;
Bright eyes through casements glances shot—
He laughed—and rode away.
The whole wide world was all aflood
With light empyrean,
And through his throbbing veins the blood
In keen sweet shudders ran.
His steed tossed head with fiery scorn,
And stamped, and snuffed the air—•
As though he heard a sudden horn
Of far-off battle blare.
Erect the rider sat awhile
With flashing eyes, and then
Turned slowly, sighing, with a smile,
“ The weary world of men !”
But ah—but ah ! the Morning glowed,
The green trees beckoned him—
And deep, and deeper still, he rode
Into the Forest Dim.
That rider with his face aglow
With joy of life I see
In dreams. Ah, years and years ago
He parted ways with me 1
Yet sometimes, when the days are drear
And all the world forlorn,
From out the dim wood’s heart I hear
The echo of his horn.
VICTOR J. DALEY.

It is over 20 years since tire birth of her previous
child.

The old law that precluded butehers from
being jurymen in murder cases, on the supposition
that their business gave them an unnatural thirst
for blood, ought to prevent any ex-butcher from
handling the prerogative of mercy.

now very feeble. He is but the extinct cinder of
the meteoric Julius of former days. His engage-
ment as London manager of a big Yankee life-
insurance office only lasted a little while.

since gaoled for selling Zola’s works in English, is
d ad. He will find the Frenchman read in both

always a dirty streak in them. Look at St.
Stead.
Brittlebank, one of the seceders from
New Australia, ought to be badly missed, for he
appears to have struck the first distinctly bright
idea developed on the settlement. He wanted to
begin work by building a commodious asylum for
lunatics.
Judge Williams’ (Vic.) knighthood comes
as a sop for bis lost Chief-Justiceship. So a
baby cries for the moon, and is appeased with a
penny rattle. Williams had a chance of showing
himself superior to Madden, but is content to be
his equal,
N.S.W. Premier Dibbs’ ambition in early
life was to be dictator of some South American
republic. He voyaged, fought and wore military
garments in that part of the world, and jokingly
remarked the other day that he “ had got there”
in his native land.
Captain Marling, who comes to N.S. W. to
drill the cavalry staff, served with Major-General
Hutton in the Soudan, This again demonstrates
that when Australia imports a high official of any
sort, it must expect, as part of the contract, to
take that official’s friends.

Sheriff Cowper (N.S.W.) knows a juror
who, in two hanging cases, discharged his duty
without a quiver, ordering the law to get “there”
on each occasion, and who, if empannelled on
larcenies or embezzlements, carries to the jury-
room a heart that bleeds copiously for the “un-
foitunate offender.”

years old, got 93 per cent, of possible marks at a
London University music exam. She practises
the piano five hours daily—playing the Caucasian
out. The Gundagai paper congratulates “our
respected townsman, Mr. Ah Chee.” N.B.—
Mr. Ah Chee has a big ad. in the Gundagai paper.

There are now in Sydney two knights
named William Manning. One is William
Montague Manning, ex-judge; the other, William
Patrick Manning, financier and mayor. It was
expected that the latter would use his second
name in connection with his title, just as Judge
Joseph George Long Innes, on his bebaublement,
suppressed the Joseph and adopted the George.
His Honor, by the way, is a good judge in more
than one sense, for that is an absolutely im-
mortal phrase : “ No offb named Joseph was ever
much of a success.”
Six members of Vic. Legislative Assembly
have died in the past year, and, as usual, Death
has a caprice on one letter, the last three being
B.’s—Burrowes, Bowman, Butterly.
When John M‘Kenzie, the bulky Maori-
la,nd Minister for Lands, came to Wellington after
his victory at Waihemo, Premier Seddon and a
crowd met him on the wharf, and it was
understood that there was to be a big demonstra-
tion. Then M‘Kenzie tramped heavily ashore,
and addressed his chief briefly with “ Halloa !”
and Seddon solemnly responded, “Halloa, John !”
That was all the ceremony.
Be the S.M. Herald cabled assertion
that Mr. Edwin Smith, K.C.M.G., of South
Australia, has been asked “by both political
parties” to stand for Wallsall (Eng.), this
altogether unprecedented demand for Edwin
Smith seems unaccountable, considering how very
little demand there ever was for him in his own
country. Apparently both parties feel that with-
out Smith they will be lost. Smith, however, has
declined, so they are lost.
James Park, a recently deceased director
of the Maoriland Shaw-Savill Co., left instruc-
tions—which were carefully carried out—that his
remains were to be cremated, and the vase con-
taining his dust was to be put aboard the first
vessel of his line sailing for Maoriland. When
the ship was off St. Catherine’s Head its bow was
to be turned exactly to the North, then the vase
was to be smashed against the ship’s side, and the
director’s ashes scattered to the winds and waves.

A well-known Southerner, interested in
Northern squatting properties, lately visited one
of his stations during the shearing, and was much
interested in the operation, which he had never
seen before. He stood watching one of the
shearers until he had finished a sheep and sent
it through the trap-door into the counting-pen.
Then the town squatter’s nationality peeped forth.
“ Vat!” said he, turning to the manager, who
was “over the board.” “do zey not take all ze
fur off ze feet!”

When Parkes is in a very tight place he
always says he “ stands in the light of the grave ”
—just as when a certain well-known Sydney
capitalist, or ex-capitalist, is in a tight place, he
invariably blows his nose in a bandanna and
winks wickedly. At Bathurst, last week, Parkes
pathetically said he did not calculate upon visiting
the city again.
“Tiger Inglis,” M.L.A., chairman of
Sydney Chamber of Commerce and ex-Minister
of Public Instruction, N.S.W., who proceeds to
England after a series of “ send-offs,” will be in
London when’his new book of Scotch stories, now
in press, is issued. Tiger’s broad-Scotch stories
are all good, and no one in Australia can tell
Scotch stories as well as he, but he isn’t telling
any Scotch broad stories in the book, nor is he
addicted to these in the measure which might be
expected of a parson’s son.
Charles E. Hern, once of Sydney, lias
just died in England, where he had a most
fashionable clientele as an art-instructor, his pupils
including the daughters of the Prince of Wales,
He was as microscopic and laborious in his art as
is theN.S.W. Attorney-General in his law. “My
microscopic friend, Mr. Heydon,” the other day
said Rogers, Q.O.
Judge Williams (Vic.), the half-and-half
sort of Radical, was partly seduced into accepting
a knighthood by the name he acquired at baptism.
Sir Hartley Williams! Who could resist the
charm of being addressed as “Sir Hartley?”
Reminds one of Sir Hartley Courtly. The Judge
has now smashed up his bicycle, one supposes.
Knights never ride such things.
FRANCES KNORR (baby-farmer, to be banged at Mel
bourne on 15th inst. for child-murder, and to avoid the
disgrace of butchering whom the Victorian hangman,
“Jones,” cut his throat last week).
Now that ex-Premier Jas. M‘Culloch is
gone, there is no barm in saying what has often
been hinted, that his marriage to Miss Inglis was
the sole cause of his political apostacy. The lady
was brought, or sent, from Scotland, Mac. being
then a widower of long duration. From the date
of his wedding he began to slant away Too-
rakianly, N.N.W., a little Southerly.
Thomas Jones, drowned in the Mitchell
River (Vic.) floods on Boxing Day, was, when a
lad, transported to Tasmania for high treason.
He was a chimney-sweep, and his master had the
Windsor Castle contract. For a joke, when the
Queen was married to Prince Albert, young Jones
crept down a flue and hid beneath the nuptial
bed. Fortunately, Victoria is a real woman, and
so before retiring she looked under the bed in the
fashion of women from time immemorial. When
she saw the young sweep, she screamed “Murder!”
A posse of lackeys ran in, and exit Mr. Jones to
Tasmania.
When Mrs. Yates, the lady Mayor (not
Mayoress) of Onehunga (M.L.), took office, the
retiring Mayor complimented her on her elevation
and hoped that she would remember the words on
the back of the Mayor’s chair ; “Be just and fear
not.” The lady retorted that she had done that
all her life, and it would be well if everybody else
could say the same. The town-clerk and the fire-
brigade have resigned, the latter sarcastically
suggesting that a female fire-brigade be formed to
match the Mayor; but Mrs. Yates “takes no
notice.” She says she is the first lady Mayor in
the British Empire, and the eyes of the civilised
world are upon her.
Bertrand, the slayer of Mr. Kinder,
whom it is sought to release from prison, where he
has been for the, in these days, unprecedented
term of a quarter of a century, was a cold-blooded,
deliberate murderer, and deserves to be kept in
gaol till he dies. Were be kept in prison until the
very end it would show that such a thing as life-
imprisonment is possible, and that there is an
effective alternative to the gallows. But, as urged
by A. G. Taylor, all men are equal before the law,
and Bertrand should be treated as other mur-
derers are treated. The private vengeance of
powerful officials should be no factor in determin-
ing the fate of even the most ruffianly criminal.

The only really independent member of
S.A. Legislature is George Ash. All the rest are
swayed from side to side by party considerations,

Premier Kingston’s enemies sneeringly
assert that he is only fitted to be President of a
South American Republic. After all, the best
work is often done by a Democracy led by an
autocrat.

Very few wealthy men now remain either
in S.A, Council or Assembly. In the former
body Angas, Simpson and Diincan are the only
plutocrats, while in the people’s- Chamber the

The N.S.W. Freetrade flute is lipped dis-
ingenuously both by Reid and the S.M. Herald.
Reid plays land-tax, not because he likes it, bub
to capture a block vote; and the Herald plays
Civil Service retrenchment, not because it likes
that, but to dish the need for land-tax, Reid
warbles to charm Georgeites, the Herald to please
land-owners ; while Freetrade herself stands
robbed of her flute.

“If I had Barton’s intellect,” said Dibbs to me once,

The judicial decision which, in the Hard
Cash case, sends Rosa and his associates for
sentence, determines the principle of one of the
points set down for argument in the lately-settled
action of N.S.W, Ministry v. Sydney Daily
Telegraph— viz., that a collection of persons
acting as one body may sue for libel. The legal
maxim that a corporation has no soul originated
with Coke, and has been a fountain of error since
his time.
Prominent Sydney politicians are now
regularly stuck up by persons who want a loan for
having called “the opponent who ran you down
at his last meeting a liar.” “You can see it in
the paper,” vouched one of these ; “ I called it to
his face and broke up the meeting.” Last week a
politician was interviewed by a hard-jawed
Stentor who for 15s. was prepared to promise
that he would not interrupt the politician in his
next harangue.
The Referendum, owing to the defeat of
Eugene J. O’Conor, who introduced it last
session, may not be carried during the present
Maoriland Parliament. O’Conor, otherwise
known as “The Bnller Lion,” for which sobriquet
he is indebted to Thatcher, had made a special
study of the Referendum, and had set his
heart on attaining it. A few years ago
he visited Switzerland, for the express pur-
pose of seeing how it worked there. The
Referendum would have stood a good chance
of passing last session, had not both Govern-
ment and Opposition whips done their best to
defeat it. A few weeks afterwards the Opposition
leader stated on the hustings that he was in favour
of it! Another instance of the absurdity of
Party Government: each side wants to have the
credit of passing a measure that is likely to be
popular, and neither one will let the other do it;
and when a private member tries to get it done
both sides “ jump on ” him.

The fact that a N.S.W. ratepayer in
arrears with rates goes bankrupt, does nob, accord-
ing to. Justice Stephen, bar the municipal council
from suing him for the amount due, the reason
appearing to be that the debt to the council has a
statutory foundation, and is thus outside the
jurisdiction of the Bankruptcy Court. If borough
councils generally choose to act on this decision, a
host of schedule filers of the last few years will
have a lively time.
The interprovincial conference of Labour
members will shortly take place in Sydney. The
principal work of the meeting will be to draw up
a platform of concerted action. In the process
the cast-iron regulations framed in Sydney
recently by the Labour-party-outside-Parliament
will come in for rough handling. On this account
Brisbane Worker opposes the conference and
threatens any Q. Labour member who dares take
part; but Queensland is to be represented all the
same.
Attorney-General Byrnes impudently says
that N.Q. Separation is dead. On the contrary,
it is only just born. The Railway Commissioners
have taken away one argument in its favour by
reducing rates on Northern lines to the scale
charged in the South, but, as long as Brisbane is
Brisbane, the other seventeen thousand reasons
will remain. This much is certain, that the British
authorities are convinced of the justice of the
demand, and only wait a conclusive plebiscite to
take action. The bias of the Southern Parliament
is thoroughly recognised, and its interested pro-
tests will go for nothing.
Military matters are mixed in Queensland.
Major-General Owen is chief of the forces, and
Lieut.-Colonel Thynne is, therefore, his subordin-
ate. But Thynne is also a member of the Cabinet,
and in that capacity he is Owen’s superior.
Therefore he can sit on the General in one capa-
city, and the General can sit on him in another.
Lately the capacities have got mixed up a good
deal through Thy»ne making a military appoint-
ment which the General promptly annulled, and
it looks as if things might end in Owen putting
the Minister under arrest, or else being sacked
himself by his own underling.
Premier Patterson’s day is surely over.
Instead of combining Agent - Generalcy with
General-Agency in the interests of economy he
appoints Duncan Gillies in the former and retains
Dr. Pearson in the latter capacity. Then, while a
few miserable village settlers are kept without
their progress - payments for improvements
effected on the holidays, on the excuse of lack of
funds, he brazenly invites Royalty to visit Vic-
toria at an expense which would more than cover
the cost of solving the present unemployed crisis.
Even the Mayor of his own district is compelled
to denounce him.
The new W.A. Constitution Bill professes
to give manhood suffrage for Assembly elections,
qualified by twelve months’ residence in the pro-
vince and six months in one district. However,
the method of application is greatly hampered,
and the matter of naturalisation of aliens is
grievously explicit. But Property can have 40
votes —there is no trouble at all about that. For
the Legislative Council, there are property quali-
fications. One of these is the simple appearance
of the_ voter’s name on the ratepayers’ roll of a
municipality or a roads board. Which gives Pro-
perty a chance to choke up the electoral-roll for
the Council with ; id or friendly dummies.

The cable reports that a French and
a British detachment have met in the dark some-
where in Sierra Leone, and mistaking each other
for an enemy they proceeded to have a breezy and
inspiriting fight. According to the British cable
the French were a trifle over two to one, and
were licked notwithstanding. How the British
cablegrammer counted them in the dark and the
general confusion isn’t explained, but it is one of
the fixed traditions of England that the English
always are fighting against heavy odds. It is 300
years since the time of the Armada, and the fact
that the Spanish fleet was the smaller of the two
in that historic ocean scrum hasn’t yet soaked
into the English comprehension. As for the fact
that the Russians were badly outnumbered at the
Alma, it hasn’t even begun to be dimly compre-
hended and isn’t likely to be this century.

CHIEFLY SPEIGHT AND SYME.


Gold discoveries. Very rich fields have
been opened at Southern Cross, Coolgardie, Mur-
chison, &c., in West Australia, which will be one
of the greatest gold-producing countries of the
world. Land in Perth and Albany, the principal
cities, is ridiculously low in price, and must cer-
tainly increase very much in value in the next
few years. One of the best investments at present
time is to secure a few lots. The Intercolonial
Investment Co., Ltd., 309 Pitt-street, Sydney,
offers exceptionally well-positioned blocks from
-filO each on very easy terms, or for cash,
guaranteed title. The company has been estab-
lished in Sydney and Perth for many years, and
is well-known throughout the colonies for straight
dealing. The manager is Mr. W. J. Douglas,
v> ho will he pleased to forward plans to any
address, and, having a personal knowledge of the
properties, will select faithfully for intending
purchasers where required.

“J. W. M‘Cutcheon, Major,” writes to
Sydney D.T. re the Yorks ;
Australia should now ask that our soil should be the
birth-place of the young prince. If this could be
granted, I venture to predict a greater consolidation of
the Empire than all the schemes that have yet been pro-

How does the Major know that it’s going to be a

has gone stark mad.
• • •
Melbournian : “ This is the queerest city
under the sun. Last Sunday night, I was stuck
up by a not penniless bushman for a fill of
tobacco, inasmuch as, near a whole cityfull, he
could get none for love or money. Near by was a
fruitshop, wide open, where I got a bottle of
lemonade. Thence I went to the Gaiety theatre,
where Will Stevens was singing a comic song to
a waltz-tune, by the band, while the collection
was being taken up in hats. The churchwardens
gave change. Will Bishop Goe try to persuade
me this was more sacred than decently taking the
money at a pay-box ? Next I visited the Rotunda
cincert, where the collection is taken at a turn-
stile, on the principle of no compulsion, but you
must. How long ere the humbug is burst up ?”
• # m
Archdeacon Farr, one of the most ancient
of Adelaide clergymen, on Boxing Day married
two couples and buried Col. Finniss and a little
girl who had been killed by an accident. Next
day he read the funeral-service over the five vic-
tims of the Oxley tragedy, and then performed a
wedding ceremony.
# * 9
Capt. Henniker, the popular bachelor
private secretary of the S. A. Lieut.-Governor, has
left Adelaide heart-whole. It is doubtful whether
he will return from England, even should Kintore
come back. He is the eldest son of a peer, and
the godson of Albert Edward, after whom he is
named.

The municipal sales of lost dogs were held
in Melb. Corporation Baths on Xmas Eve
last year. A reserve of one shilling is laid on each
animal; those not purchased are thrown into an
iron cage which is submerged in water later on.
NO FALSE PRIDE.
LUGGINS (ironically, to Boozer who is waiting fora shout): “ I supposeit’s
no good asking you to join us in a drink?”
BOOZER: “Oh, I don’t mind. I ain’t particular. I’d drink with a
blanky blackfellow.”
A big Newfoundland would be led forward and
immediately snapped up. Then a roguish-looking
little mongrel would face the audience wagging
his tail as if to say, “Do buy me, somebody !”
but it would be no go and off he would be dragged
to his happy Xmas. The skins of the slaughtered
are not used—the animals are buried intact.
, The silly-season discussion for this year in
Melb. Argus is on the Australian accent as dis-
covered by Chief Justice Madden. It is rather
amusing to see J. L. Purves picked out by one of
the correspondents to the controversy as the model
speaker. Purves has a decided Australian accent,
as a matter of fact. He invariably says “ slistor”
for “solicitor,” and “ t waive” for “twelve.”
When all is said and done, it is merely a matter
of prevailing custom that determines the correct
enunciation of the language.
Even Bishop Moorhouse, whom most people
here considered a perfect Chrysostom in voice and
eloquence, is said by those “in the know ” to lack
the final polish of “ English culture,” and ex-Gov.
Loch positively gabbles. So it is not only we
poor “colonials” who sin against correct speech.
The Honolulu natives, who draw their “ English”
from the pure well of ’Frisco water-front, mimic
and laugh at the “British accent ”of the mail-
steamer tourists. Where our national twang
comes out particularly strong, however, is not in
talking, but in singing. Even in some churches
you could out the accent of the choir with a knife—
when, for instance, they request the congregation

A back-blocks N.S.W. lady writes The
Bulletin for advice. Her case is peculiar In
an ill-advised moment, several snake-seasons’ ago
s h e sucked poison from the leg of a boy who had
been bitten by a large black snake. He recovered
and published a letter giving her the credit This
got her name up, and the relatives of snake-bitten
residents have ever since been in the habit of
requisitioning her (so to speak) professional ser-
vices. The climax came a few days since, when a
deputation of his countrymen rushed into her
house with a particularly filthy Chinaman, par-
tially undressed him on the sofa, and loudly
demanded suction for his elbow. Just here the
lady writes for advice; and, as far as we can
gather, the bite is waiting.

Dear Bulletin, —Anent statist Hayter’s
“ artificial crime ” of travelling first-class on a
second-class ticket, here’s a little yarn. Tickets
on the Brighton line used to be only occasionally
examined, and one night in a smoking-carriage a
well-known Bustopolis Calicroesus had all the
talk. “ The man who travels * first ’on a ‘second’
ticket is a thief, sir ! —neither more nor less ! If I
had my way, I’d give him two years’ hard ! ”
Unexpected guard at Richmond station: “Tickets,
please ! ” And the curtain fell to roars of laughter
as the lofty moralist unwillingly produced—a
second-class ticket! He had reason to be glad,
next day, that it was a £2— and not a two-year
matter ! —Yio.
» * *
In Maoriland, when anybody developes
into an incurable drunk, the law issues a prohibi-
tion order, and thereafter any publican supplying
him with drink incurs heavy penalties. The
other day, at Auckland, a serious old teetotal lady
applied for a prohibition order against herself.
Being asked if she was an incurable drinker, she
indignantly replied that she never tasted liquor
in any form, and only wanted the order issued
“on principle ” and as a good example to others.
The order was issued accordingly, and when she
found that her name would be duly posted up in
all hotels with the dipsomaniacs and the obscene
language men, she departed satisfied.

Dunedin (M.L.) has started a Social
Reform Association on the good old Social
Gimlet principle. Members of the Association
are to go round stealthily and hold their breath
while they listen with a large, holy ear at other
people’s keyholes, and are to look with a sanctified
eye through cracks in the weatherboards. A
special watch is to be kept—from the highest
motives—upon bedroom windows —especially the
windows of ladies’ bedrooms—where the blind
has been left up by accident; and any man out
walking after dark with a woman is to be tracked
and (if not too large) admonished.
The good, snnfflesome city of Adelaide had a
similar society a few years ago, but some of its
members were caned, and some were kicked, and
some were rubbed against orderly-bins, and at
last the society became discouraged. The worst
thing that happened to it, however, was never
published. An enthusiastic member, acting on
THE AUSTRALIAN SONGSTRESS.
What time suburban gas is lit,
The girl suburban lifts her song.
Her notes through various mazes flit
In vocal breathings sweet and strong ;
But listeners bored it merely riles
To hear her sing of nightingyles.
Her song it soareth up and down—
“ Will crowds some day to hear her flock?**
We murmur ; hope for her renown.
But sudden feel a short, sharp shock ;
She tells of funeral flower-device,
“ White lilies round a pyle, pylefyce.”
’Tis of a fiddle now she sings,
Which could her want of home supply,
Treasured ’mid early wanderings
O’er heath and moor, by night and dye,
This instrument so blythe and gye
Entwined among her heart-strings lye.
But not alone the letter A
In vocal twists she turns and frets.
Her other vowels get astray
When in her hurry she forgets.
“ How can I love and bid thee gow i ’ t
She asks, and echo answers “ How ?'*
O, maiden with the sweet young voice 1
If local accent thee beguile,
Pause, make thy hearers’ hearts rejoice,
Take counsel with thyself awhile ;
’Twere better if thou never sang
Than voiced it in Australian twang.
LOVE A LA MODE.
’Tis the youth who cuts a dash
With a nicely waxed moustache,
A tender tenor voicelet and an air of mysteree,
A yard of curling love-locks and a ton of poetree,
’Tis he who makes her flutter
With a joy no tongue can utter,
’Tis he who makes her fancy, when he leaves her at
eleven,
That there’s only tissue-paper ’twixt her little soul and
heaven.
’Tis the man of stately port,
He whose nose hath many a wart,
He who has a bloated bank-book and a villa by the sea,
He who hath a paunch capacious and a store of £ s. d.,
Tho’ his out be somewhat careless,
Tho’ his pate be smooth and hairless,
’Tis he who scoops the maiden in, despite the scoffer’s
laughter,
’Tis he who bears her home and makes her happy ever
after.
P. LUFTIG.
ARENEWAL.
Another strip of the Age-sward bare 1
A blighted swathe to reckon !
And while Time whets his scythe, we swear
This time we’ll keep in good repair,
Our yearly vow — to coldly stare
When 'VTce or Folly beckon.
Dec. 31, 1893.
FRANK BEM.MAN.
GALTON’S BOOK.
The intellectual life of Mr. Oalton. private secretary to
N.S.W. Governor Duff, has been (solemnly writes J.
Tighe Ryan in a wondronsly funny Sydney D.T. article)
somewhat romantic. There is a story in' circulation to
the effect that when he was waltzing in a ballroom last
season it was noticed that he had something bulky inside
his vest. The mystery, which was the occasion of much
gossip, was understood later on when the private secre-
tary was seen in a quiet corner of the room reading a
Greek Homer.
Gallon, hero of the life romantic,
Dancers in a ball-room once drove frantic,
Not with airs, or intellectual “jam,”
But with a swelling ’neath his diaphragm.
But our Gallon nimbly cut his capers,
Heedless of the crowd of Potts’ Point drapers,
Thinking only of Achilles’ foes,
Or of ancient father Priam’s woes.
Soon, unseen, a quiet corner finds him,
Reading Homer’s Greek where no one minds him,
While his vest, deliver’d of the book,
Smooth, no more can draw the curious look.
Whisper, Gallon unsophisticated,
Poetry and ball-rooms are not mated,
Next time leave your Homer on the shelf,
That, good youth, or stay at home yourself!
E.E.

The official report of the aboriginal camp
at Boggabilla, near Narrabri, N.S.W., presented
a few days ago to the Aborigines Protection Board,
sitting in Folice-luspector-Ceneral Fosbery’s Syd-
ney office, stated that “drunkenness was decidedly
on the increase, and gambling rampant at the
camp,” and the minutes of the meeting record
that the Board unanimously resolved that the
report was “satisfactory.”

Melb. W. T. C. U. is making things quite easy
for the Almighty. Through its instrumentality 7
persons were fined at Richmond the other day for
selling pennyworths of lollies and fruit on the
Lord’s day. The youthful customers in each case
had embezzled coppers meant for the heathen.
A pity the law concerns itself with the petty
jealousies of these rival business concerns.
• * *
Sydney and its suburbs are now infested
with the man of the soundless footfall. He who
overtakes or abruptly confronts you, coming out
of the dark without a premonitory sound of heel
or toe, is an assailant in method. A police regu-
lation prohibits the use of noiseless boots by the
force, although, in many cases, constables might
turn such footgear to public advantage; and the
reason which supports the regulation would
justify making the wearing of such boots by others
an offence.
* * *
Australian Star, reprobating the Yic.
women’s petition for the commutation of the
death-sentence on Frances Knorr, says :
It is curious that the crime of child-murder, to judge
by this document, as well as by the sex of those who
generally commit this crime, is regarded much more
lightly by women than by men. Yet, surely, it should be
otherwise.
This is another allusion to the maternity senti-
ment; but the graver point is that the Star
implies that women, if they differ from men on
this matter, do so without cause. Child-murder
is, however, very often the human consequence of
man’s iniquity ; the woman-culprit is tried in a
court presided over by a man, is prosecuted by
men, and judged by men alone. Does not all this,
and more, constitute a prima facie reason for
womanly interference between the sentence and
its fulfilment ?

Melbourne is infested by housebreakers,
ir shopbreakers, whose chief ambition is to burgle
beer and tobacco. No private citizen possessed
Df much portable property thinks of going to bed
before he has placed a dozen of ale and a box of
figara on the dining-room table. That anchors
the ruffians to the one apartment, until they
eventually stagger off too drunk to carry away
more than is inside them.

Since 1860 (33 years) the American con-
gress has appropriated to rail-roads Government
land to the amount of 3,000,000,000 acres. Much
of this land is valuable, and was valuable when
given to railroads. Put the 3,000,000,000 acres at
$5 per acre, and we have the sum of $15,000,000,000,
which would more than pay for all the railroads
in the United States, watered stock and all; while
the actual cost of the roads has not been above
$7,000,000,000 or $8,000,000,000, the stock has
been watered to perhaps $15,000,000,000. Now
give the man thunder who says we cannot buy
the roads.—Progressive Farmer.
Jabez Spencer Balfour, the British holy
man who ran away with the millions of the
Liberator Society, doesn’t seem to be having a
good time in Argentina. He lives in a gorgeous
mansion, and a cold sweat of terror, at Lamos de
Zomora, 25 miles from Buenos Ayres, and his
short, circular figure is never seen outside the
front door of the premises except under a strong
guard. The house is constantly watched by over
100 police and soldiers, and Jabez’s hard-stolen
money is fast melting away in paying for these
elaborate defences. Very soon, as things go, he
will be a short, wheezy, red-nosed beggar in the
streets of Buenos Ayres, and his present satellites
will run him in for vagrancy and having no p’ace
to sleep. Then, if the British Government wants
him it can lure him on board ship by holding up
a sixpence or a fried chop, and crooking its finger
at him.

Millionaire Tyson :
The men of central Australia will be among the picked
men of the world, physically and mentally ; the horses
will be the fleetest and most enduring; the cattle and
sheep will yield the most tender, succulent and tooth-
some meat. England is a manure heap whose soil is
poisoned by the animal droppings of centuries, and whose
vegetation is less wholesome for stock than the health-
giving natb e grasses of inland Australia, with pastures
renovated by hush fires and purified by a tropical sun.
Tyson is right enough. England is “worked
over and over with sour, dead and distempered
corpses.” Under her soil are the foul liquid and
meat of the drunkards and gluttons of countless
generations. Her best fruit suggests the putres-
cence it springs from. The lush, rich grass- the
stock rolling fat-hearty John Bull himself,
coarse, full of blood, bull-necked, apoplectic-all
are ripe with an unhealthy rankness. Looking a*
an English girl—clean, sane, and lusciofis—one
involuntarily says with Walt Whitman: “Now
I am terrified at the Earth ! it is that calm and
patient. It grows such sweet things out of such
corruptions.”
Editor Bulletin,— Re “The Ophir of
Charles de Breuil ” in your issue of 23rd Dec. Hero
is a clipping from a London daily of several years
ago
M. Baudoin, a doctor who went out to New Ireland
with one of the expeditions organised by M. de Breuil
better known as the Marquis des Rays, now awaiting his
trial on some grave charges, gives some very interesting
details as to the manner in which Maragnane, the native
King, signed the treaty presented by Captain Rabardy.
His Majesty was invited on board the Genii, and appeared
on the quarter-deck in the full, but not complete, costume
of a band of red cloth round his arm and a reed through his
nose. He was accommodated with a seat on a hencoop, and
wasplaced within a tempting distance of a scarlet petticoat,
a bunch of clay pipes, a quantity of tobacco, and some
beads. His intention was so taken up by the munificence
of Rabardy that he took little or no notice of the hens
which pecked irreverently at his sable legs every now and
again. He was asked if the island belonged to him, and
answered in the affirmative, making for the presents. He
was forced back into his seat, while the lawyer who
accompanied the expedition read over the charter by
which the white man had everything given to him. He
was told he could have the presents if he signed the docu-
ment. With tears in his eyes, he urged his ignorance of
the art of caligraphy, a:«l the lawyer guided his hand,
showing him to make a cross. His courtiers who had
accompanied him looked on with surprise. When they
saw that making a mark with a pen placed their monarch
in possession of so many rich gifts, they came forward
with touching unanimity, and Captain Rabardy had some
difficulty in preventing them from covering the deed with
crosses.
SARDONYX.
“In proportion as a man is an artist, he
will not be a critic*.”
Judge Williams, in response to the con-
gratulations of the Melbourne Bar on his receipt of
an Imperial tag : “ I feel honoured by the dis-
tinction conferred upon me by the Queen and
regard it as a recognition of any small service I
may have rendered the State in the high and
responsible office I hold.” The Judge, in wearing
the Imperial bauble, advertises his opinion that
the people he serves for a fat salary are not in a
position to adequately reward him for his services.
A democracy cannot afford to keep this class of
servant, and it doesn’t when it knows itself.
Dear Bulletin, —For 25 consecutive years,
it seems, a N.S.W. Governor had not failed to
turn up at the Sydney Caledonian Society’s
dinner, from which fact it is assumed by a
vociferous mob of transplanted Scotchmen that
their compatriot, Duff, disregarded a solemn duty
when he kept away from that heavy, clannish
feed. In truth, a Governor, presuming him to
have any business anywhere in these parts, has no
business at the assertive racial demonstration in
question. Neither ought he to attend a Celtic
national gorge, or a Welsh-rabbit supper, or half-
a-dozen other festivals whereat the chairman
“ blows ” concerning the superiority of his native
heath, ould sod, or mountain ranges for human
breeding purposes. You are frequently severe,
upon_ England, but you must at least admit that
Englishmen in Australia, or anywhere else, don’t
form themselves into a party for purposes of
defence and offence against their neighbours. As
for the Caledonians of Sydney, they have publicly
asserted a commanding claim upon vice-regality.
Scotchmen are always to be specially glorified
through bis Excellency, the Governor. Please
make a note of this and oblige a— British
Radical.
Newcastle, N.S.W., supplies a grim
domestic tragedy. Mrs. Hyde, a resident of
Young Wallsend (a deserted mining township
outside Newcastle), had a husband of unexampled
brutality, whose bestial cruelty made her life
almost insupportable. She repeatedly sought the
protection of the law against him, and, though
after having him imprisoned twice, she obtained a
legal separation, he frequently forced his way
into her residence and inhumanly assaulted her.
At last local Quarter Sessions he was tried for an
unmentionable offence upon her, and though the
medical evidence thoroughly confirmed hers, he
was discharged. Emboldened by this escape, he
repeated his acts of swinish cruelty. About a
fortnight ago he accidentally met her in the lonely
bush near Wallsend, and, after maltreating her
for four hours in the pouring rain, left her, bruised,
helpless and unable to walk, by the side of the
railway, where some hours after she was
discovered by two of her young children.
A few nights ago he came to her house drunk,
slept for a few hours, then arose and began to
drag her round the floor by the hair. The un-
fortunate woman, driven to desperation by this
and other cruel indignities, shot him through the
temple with a revolver which she had purchased
to protect herself after her last ineffectual appeal
for justice. She then surrendered herself to the
police, first sending for a medical man to treat
her still living husband, who died a few hours
after. The greatest sympathy is felt for the un-
fortunate woman, who was respected for her
brave attempts to rear her family by continuous
hard work, and pitied for the terrible suffering
she was compelled to undergo at her husband's
hands. It is said by the neighbours that some of
the acts he subjected her to were incredible in
their devilish ingenuity. At the inquest which
followed the jury brought in a verdict of justifiable
homicide in defence of life and the woman was
accordingly discharged from custody.
MY PHILOSOPHY.
Whether it’s sickness, troubles,
Or fortune’s reverse,
I think of poor others, and —
“ Well, it might have been worse.”
HENRY ANDERSON.
PETITION TO THE SECRETARY OF
STATE.
Recall this canny, kilted chiel,
Recall him hame by cable—
He spurned a tasty Scottish meal
Wi’ Scotties at the table.
The Lord preserve our native heath !
And Heaven bless its heather,
And grant that proud Sir Robert’s teeth
May drop out altogether !
No mongrel Sassenach rebuff
Would trouble Mac or Archie,
But deevil tak the doughty Duff
Who treats his brithers starchy.
Our fathers fought at Prestonpans,
They also bled wi’ Wally,
And insults to our Hieland clans
We oanna’ stand, by golly !
Then drag him fra’ his billet braw;
A chieftain o’ his kidney,
According to our Scottish law,
Is no the mon for Sydney.
We wadna’ wish to see him dee
Of patriotic vi’lence,
But when he cuts his lucky we
Will cheer in solemn silence.

Poor little Dr. Renwick, of Sydney, has,
it seems, been definitely promised his long-sought
bauble. One cable-agency postpones the coveted
degradation until next May 24. If that be so, the
little man is probably running after Her Gracious
with a stethoscope to test her chances of safely
negotiating another birthday. Renwick ought
certainly to have his own carcase sounded, so that,
in case of alarming symptoms, he may be flat-
tened out with the blade of Her Gracious’s sword,
ere the dews of death damp his miserable little
brow. Anyway, across 12,000 miles of water,
Australians hoar the front door of the Colonial
Office bang and click, and the reverberations of a
chorus of—Thank God ! we’ve got rid of him at
last !”
Dear Bulletin,— Why do the papers speak
of the coming “wet season” on Coolgardie?
Possibly in March and October there may be some
atmospherical disturbances, such as are common,
at that period, all over coastal Australia, but these
disturbances may, or may not (probably not),
reach as far inland as Coolgardie. Coolgardie is
on the margin of the great inland area, whereon
the annual rainfall averages sin. and under, which
means a fair chance that no rain may fall for
years. To speak in such a light and airy manner
of “ the wet season,” as though one were speaking
of tropical Queensland, is absurd. The physical
formation of the surface is an everlasting evidence
of the amount of rainfall to be reckoned on with
any reasonable degree of certainty, and that
amount is simply x. The “ wet season ”on Cool-
gardie is all a gamble.
Gladstone was 84 on 29th Dec. Despite
gush over his “unimpaired vigour of mind and
body,” the old man (The Bulletin gets this from
a recent observer) has aged a great deal during
last year. He is grown much thinner—his
form seems shrunken to half what it was
three years ago. His voice, too, is dulled and
husky, and is heard with difficulty at ordinary
times. Only fanatical admirers refuse to admit
that his mental faculties are failing fast. But
(lacking the “commonplace ” wisdom of the truly
sapient sculler, Beach) he will persist in keeping
the field until the very last. Perhaps, however,
this is to his party’s interest, for there is none to
replace him. Gladstone is a magnificent tradition,
but his day for deeds is over. Now, it is cabled
that he is ill, and that the doctors diagnose
tumour.
Now we see the genesis of the Cable
Copyright Bill lately introduced into N.S.W.
A ssembly by Wan t, Q. C., on behalf of certai n people
who control what they call the “ great dailies ” of
Australasia. These gentlemen, with a view of
instituting a monopolistic and comparatively
cheap cable-service, lately combined and agreed
to discontinue one of the rival services in which a
lot of nose-cutting has been done to spite their
own faces. It was to secure to the ring the full
and exclusive benefit of their own messages that
they promoted the bill in question, which bill was
so derisively thrown out of N.S.W. Assembly.
The effect of the measure would have been to
render utterly impossible any effective opposition;
so that, as a matter of fact, the association would
not only have had a monopoly of cable-news,
but also what amounted to a monopoly of the
right of printing daily newspapers. Much to the
chagrin of the big combine, the Australian Star,
Melbourne Herald, and Tasmanian News stood
out, and they have now joined in establishing a
new service, which is working splendidly, and
which last week anticipated the morning paper
services by 12 or 16 hours in a good many im-
portant items. Readers of evening newspapers
have long since been thoroughly sick of the
“daily” paper cable-service, which always con-
veyed the idea that the evening papers had been
cribbing from the morning press. As it is in
the public interest that there should be no news-
paper monopoly The Bulletin is glad to notice
the success of the new service.

Labour gets the Capital.”
Death from nail-biting is one of the grue-
some possibilities of this especially lamentable
sphere. Recently, in England, a girl died of an
obscure and unfixable disease, and th o post-mortem
revealed that the cause of death was the accumu-
lation of nail-ends in the intestines.
FOUR HOURS TOO LATE.
“ Oh, God I if I could recall the past three or four
hours. See what trouble a man may bring upon
himself all in a moment .”
Mr. James Curtis and Mr. C. S. Bennett, both
of San Francisco, had been intimate friends for
years. Last summer they quarrelled for the first
time, each accusing the other of wrong. Better
thoughts prevailed and they were reconciled.
But you can’t undo what is once done. Memory
kept the record on her slate. They avoided each
other as much as possible. Still, living as neigh-
bours, men must meet some time. These two met
in the street. The quarrel was renewed. Bitter
words flew fast and thick, and Curtis, beside him-
self with rage, drew his pistol and shot Bennett
dead on the spot. Later in the day he used the
above language—so laden with self-reproach and
sorrow. Yet how useless, how hopeless, how
vain.
But was Curtis right in saying that trouble may
come all in a moment ? True, it often seems so,
but is there not a deeper fact which we don’t see?
It matters nothing what the nature of the trouble
is. Therefore, Jet us consider a different case on
the same principle.
An intelligent woman says:—“ln October,
1890, I had an attack of illness from which I never
expected to recover. I had aching pains all over
me and a cough that nearly shook me to pieces.
I obtained no good sleep night or day, and had to
take to ray bed. I was fed with liquid food from
an invalid’s cup, for I could not raise myself in
bed. My heart fluttered so you could hear it beat
on the pillow, and often pain struck through it as
though somebody had stabbed me. I lay perfectly
helpless and could scarcely breathe. A doctor
attended me over a month, but I grew weaker and
weaker. Sometimes at night I was so bad he
feared I would not live till morning. He called in
a consulting physician and both agreed that my
condition was critical. I was fed with brandy to
keep me alive. My husband and daughter stayed
with me almost constantly. None of the medicines
administered had any effect. I was almost at
death’s door.
“At this time, Mrs. Keeling, of Mutley, near
Plymouth, a friend of mine, urged me to try a
medicine called Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup.
I procured a bottle, and after a few days I was
able to take and digest sufficient food to give me
some strength, and the worse symptoms were
greatly abated. After having used six bottles of
the Syrup my health was completely restored, and
I have since felt better than for the previous thirty
years. My two daughters have also been cured
of indigestion by it. I will gladly answer any
enquiries.’’
(Signed) Mrs. Louise Jackson, Builders’ Arms
Hotel, Bridge Road, Hammersmith, London,
Jan. 11th, 1892.
In the letter, from which the above is an extract,
Mrs. Jackson further states that for over twenty
years before the attack of October, 1890, she had
suffered from a disordered stomach and liver.
She had a bad taste in the mouth, a poor appetite,
and what little she ate gave her pain. She felt
dull, languid, and tired, and had a miserable
sinking feeling in the stomach, great pain in the
chest and sides, palpitation, giddiness, and fre-
quently fell in the street and had to be assisted
I home.
So we perceive that in her case a cause, long in
operation, at last produced the crisis which came
near ending her life. It is always thus, whether
we recognise the cause or not. The crime com-
mitted by Curtis was the sudden passionate act of
a than who allowed thoughts of hate and vengeance
to take possession of his mind and breed the con-
! dition which made murder possible. In the very
different case of this lady’s illness it was an enemy
; of her body, indigestion and dyspepsia, which at
length broke out into violence.
The lesson is the same. Watch the beginning
of evil and check it while yet it may be easily
controlled.

Instead of Spending Thousands of Pounds annually in advertising. The National Drug Coy., Lave
decided upon a new departure in the way of pushing the sale of their wonderful “ Star Antihilious
Pills”.—From January 1st, 1894 to December 31st, 1894, a distribution of Three-fourths of the net
profits derived from the sale of their pills will he divided amongst their customers. —First division of
profits on 31st March, future divisions at end of June, September and Decern oer. Each box of Pills will
bear a separate number, and every person sending Fifteen Penny Stamps for a box of Pills will
be entitled to participate in the current quarterly division of profits.—Dividend Cheques to be made out
in sums of £5 each and posted immediately on our receipt of winning numbers. Successful numbers also
names and addresses of holders will be advertised on following week in Australasian, Town dr’ Country
Journal and S. A. Observer. —The strictest impartiality to be observed and fairness of division un-
questionable.—Present Profits average £1090 per annum, so that with the large increase of business
resiilting from this announcement it is only reasonable to anticipate that at least £200 will be available
fqr each Quarterly distribution. Please note number on Box and compare same with advertised
list after cud of quarter.—Addrkss— National Drug Coy., Russell Street, Melbourne.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Goddard v. Laing fight was the most
tremendous affair ever known in Melb. So prodigious
was the shouting that people were drawn right from
Bourke-street, fancying a political meeting was on. They
thought it was the Anarchists, but it was only the
Anakim. The crowd jammed out equalled that jammed
in.
# * *
It is an odoriferous practice for race-meeting
stewards to adjudicate upon questioned events, in the
results of which they are largely interested, their heavy
wagers being matters of common notoriety. The only
quasi-excuse possible is that the purification of one small
tentacle can’t cleanse the whole sponge saturated with
fraud. _ _
» * •
E. Jones, the S. A. fast bowler, has a moustache
almost entirely white on one side ; this, coupled with
sundry patches of white hair, gives the lightning trundler
quite a venerable appearance when viewed from that side.
Jones formerly played for a Broken Hill club, where, on
bumpy asphalt pitches, he laid out nearly as many bats-
men as wickets. At South Broken Hill, the local record
hath it, while bowling in a match, he once sent a bail hi
yards behind the wickets.
* * *
Aliok Bannerman for the first time left out of
an Interprovinoial eleven.
# # «
That ultra - astute pair, the Hon. William
Alexander Long and Mat Harris made a coup on a
moderate scale in the Kogarah Stakes at Moorefleld on
Saturday. It is safe to say not a soul on the ground, bar
the horse’s people, had a wager on Gerard, who started at
100 to 9, and won in a canter with Mat looking round at
his field.
* • •
Newell’s catching of Lyons in the slips on
Saturday, at Sydney, was an eye-opener. The eye could
toircely follow the ball, and it was not till the third
attempt that Newell finally held it.
tt # *
It seemed to The Bulletin unfair to give the
high diving championship to Parkinson, at the recent
gala at Lady Robinson’s Beach. Yon Hammer went off
the same height, but it wai given to Parkinson on the
ground that his was the cleanest dive. As well might
a decision be given, where two men swim a dead-
heat, in favour of the man who swims in the best style.
* # #
The high dive is a dangerous game at the best
of times, but it is rendered trebly so at the Beach by the
men taking off from a rioketty, ill-stayed pole.
tt * *
An outsider would think it would pay the pro-
prietary of a oertai i Sydney pony-course to make a severe
example now and then. It would make the finishes more
exciting and increase speculation.
The playing of “ Billy ” Murdoch with the
N.S.W. team is, no doubt, a trump-card as regards
“ gate." Had it not been for that, the South Australians
would, without doubt, have objected to his playing as a
N.S.W. representative.
• • •
Great sport, pony-racing. Lately one punter,
on two occasions, heavily backed a pony, and ‘‘deadened ”
all he thought dangerous. On the first occasion an un-
thought-of one beat his elect, and, in the other, when one
of the stiffened riders tried to get hold of his horse, he
found that some ruffian having greased the reins, he
couldn’t get sufficient hold to keep his mount back.
tt • *
Strong-man Sandow’s system consists “essen-
tially in the acquirement of perfectly voluntary control
over the various groups of muscles, and, where possible,
of single muscles. By this specialising he was enabled to
localise his muscle-building where it appeared to him to
ba most needed. Having acquired a fair development
and control of the group selected, he then devoted his
attention to another, and so on, until he had succeeded in
acquiring the foundation of the remarkable • general
development which lie nows presents. The only apparatus
used was a pair of five-pound dumb-bells night and morn-
ing."
tt » •
The cricket-match, N.S. Wales v. South Aus-
tralia, ended on Tuesday with a hollow victory for
N.S.W. by one innings and 153 runs. South Australia
won the toss and went in to make a comparatively feeble
innings of 117 runs. Blinman (26), Lyons (20), Jarvis
and W. Qiffen (13 each), were the only scores
which reached two figures, barring that of an
energetic player called “Sundries,” who made 14.
The day being nearly over and the wicket uncertain,
Moses, the N.S.W. skipper, then judiciously sent in the
tail of his team first, and kept the best men for Monday
morning, when they went in on a reasonably good wicket
and made a brilliant score of 393. Moses (104),
Iredale (64), Callaway and Newell (CO each), supplied
moat of this total. The South Australian men were
apparently demoralised by these figures, and
their second innings only amounted to 118. George
Qiffen, with 35, was top scorer. In the first innings of the
S.A. players Newell took three wickets for 13 runs, and in
the second innings Coningham did most destruction,
taking six wickets. The S.A. bowling broke down badly,
Jarvis’ four wickets for 95 runs being the best record.
“The character of a nation is not to be
learned from its fine folks.”—Sir Walter Scott.
At the big revival meeting : “ Won’t you
come and be saved, brother?” “No; I’m a
reporter." “Oh ! beg pardon.”

The Argus hesitates at adopting that heading

Marie Corelli’s latest, “ Barabbas,” is a
queer sort of novel. Judas had a sister named
Judith, mistress of High Priest Caiaphas. Under
Oaiaphas’ influence, Judith persuades Judas that
the betrayal will precipitate the action of Jesus,
in making himself king, and be thus a praise-
worthy act—a view suggested by De Quincey,
When the crashing failure comes, Judith suicides,
as well as Judas. Our parson says the ladies were
first at the sepulchre.
He Stout’s declaration that the minority
must bow to the majority on the liquor-prohibition
question, a jeering elector asked him whether, if
the majority wished it, he would be in favour of
the male population simultaneously omitting to
wear trousers. Stout said certainly, if it could be
shown that trousers did as much harm as whisky.
A/n-o/m, when Jack Hamilton, M.L.A., Aus-
tralia’s champion revolver-shot, was doctoring on
the Palmer rush, and beat all records for getting
over the ground, he habitually concealed his
medical skill under nothing but a shirt and a hat;
and, when it was very hot, he didn’t bother about
the shirt.
The widely-spread rumour that millionaire
M'Calrnont is to marry the Duchess of Marl-
borough has been contradicted by the gentleman.
As there is never smoke without fire it may
turn out that the lucky lady is the mother of
the young Duke of Marlborough—the Marchioness
of Blandford—who divorced her husband, the late
duke. She is a charming woman. M'Cahnont is
a widower. How is it, by-the-bye, that widows
draw so many matrimonial prizes ?
Considering the ghastly mess which
Sydney School of Arts committee has made of the
finances of the institution, they should allow the
appointment of a new secretary to devolve on
their successors, who will be elected in a few
weeks. Of course nobody is responsible. The
auditors, who got a few pounds each annually,
were not supposed to check the accounts, but only
to certify that the secretary’s statement was pro-
perly added up, or something. The whole busi-
ness turns on the same old problem : “ What are
the duties of an auditor? What are the duties of
a board of directors ?”
Judge Richmond (M.L.) was sitting rather
violently the other day on a very small and
crushed-looking bankrupt, for not keeping his
books in proper order, when the broken one’s
lawyer revolted. He gently pointed out that
even his Honor might break suddenly through
unavoidable misfortune, and, supposing he did,
how would his Honor’s books look? His Honor
spent a few seconds in flabbergasted reflection,
and then admitted that his books would probably
look remarkably bad, and after that he dealt a
little more gently with the depressed insolvent.

Fate.
Two shall be born the whole wide world apart.
And speak in different tongues, and have no thought
Each of the other’s being, and no heed ;
Yet these o’er unknown seas to unknown lands
Shall cross; escaping wreck, defying death,
And all unconsciously shape every act
And bend each wandering step unto this end.
That one day out of darkness they shall meet,
And read life’s meaning in each other’s eyes.
And two shall walk some narrow way of life
So closely side by side, that should one turn
Ever so little space to left or right,
They needs must stand acknowledged face to face ;
Yet these with groping hands that never clasp,
With wistful eyes that never meet, and lips
Calling in vain on ears that never hear,
Shall wander ail their weary day unknown
And die unsatisfied. And this is Fate 1

Precedent. — lgnorance up to date.
A S k * lis Saturday’s Argus 12 pages—
A “Lady Complexionist ” now regularly
advertises in the Melb. papers. What is wanted
by Melb. ladies is a “ pedallist.” Freckles can be
disguised, but feet are so obtrusive.
The Northern Territory Times says that
the once-extensive local herds of buffalo are now
all but extinct. Government has been urged to
prohibit further shooting during next season.
The Law, says J udge Hodges, has drawn
certain definitions of libel which are more acade-
mical than practical. The Law’s definitions of
most things are likewise. Practical law would
mean cheap litigation and few lawyers.
The female franchise—a woman’s reason.
At the recent Maoriland general election, a lady
objected to vote for a certain candidate because he
spoke familiarly of her husband as “Jim,” when
conversing with her. His proper name was
Mister.
“ Vanity Pair ” fact from the Argus : “A
Welsh miner invariably secures as many sons as
he possibly can, since, with a boy to fill the trucks
for him after he has got out the coal, he can
double his earnings.” No remarks.
Dear Bulletin, — He snakes swallowing
their young. While digging the other day 1
turned up a 4in. centipede. It had a nest of
young ones, looking for all the world like grains
of uncooked rice in colour and size, but each had
its legs and other members complete. No sooner
had 1 exposed the nest than the mother at once
began swallowing the young ones, and in less than
two minutes the whole lot, numbering 18, were
safely tucked away inside. —Yours, Northern
TERRITORY,
The crew of the barque, Spirit of the
Dawn, wrecked on the barren Antipodes Islands,
spent 87 days on that desolate spot before they
were rescued by the Hinernoa, a few weeks ago.
“hey were quite unaware that an ample supply
of provisions had been placed on the island by
the Maoriland Government for the relief of ship-
wrecked people and “planted ” so safely that no one
dropped across them. The profoundly careful
fashion in which these provisions were concealed
was about the most gruesome part of the story.
A man might have starved to death right within
a few feet of them, never dreaming of their
existence.
The Bishop of Chichester (England) re-
cently delivered a lecture on the congenial subject
of Thrift, which lecture has been much approved
by the Tory press. It was garnished with two
anecdotes. One concerned a Sussex labourer
earning 13s. a week, and often less, who, by the
simple process of saving Is. a week, died worth
.£900. He passed away at the age of 72, and left
.£100 to each of his nine children. The other yarn
related to an agricultural labourer who received
16s, a week, while in the flesh—if there ever was
any flesh on him—and died leaving ,£2000, all the
result of rigid economy. The poor old bishop,
guiltless of arithmetic, evidently never
stopped to consider that it would have taken the
first clodhopper something like 200 years, even
allowing for compound interest, to get his .£900
together, and that the other alleged person only
earned £2000 altogether in a full half-century.
Bishops are awful liars.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
17 of this issue.
In his recently-issued Australian novel,
“Out Back,” Kenneth Mackay works in a lot of
very much stereotyped characters including “ the
weary bushranger ’’—for whose appearance he
makes a prefatory apology—but he has carefully
omitted the wattle, the trade-mark of Australian
literature. He would have done much better had
he played a lone hand and left to the English
writers of Australian fiction the traditional char-
acters and incidents which figure in his present
work. The writer of “ Stirrup Jingles ” should be
able to strike new paths and create new actors.
In “ Out Back ” he shows his knowledge of the
true inwardness of Australia and the inhabitants
thereof and his work throughout shows that he
has seen or lived—bushranging excepted—and be-
come letter perfect in the scenery of which he writes.
The plot shows much more cleverness in construc-
tion than skill in presentation, and the last few
chapters give an anyhow and slovenly finish to the
yarn. Kenneth Mackay is a word-artist in
the best sense of the word, but he would
do a lot better if he worked just a trifle more care-
fully.
A TRIOLET.
Of all the sickly forms of verse,
Commend me to the triolet.
It makes bad writers somewhat worse :
Of all the sickly forms of verse
That fall beneath a reader’s curse.
It is the feeblest jingle yet.
Of all the sickly forms of verse
Commend me to the triolet.

The Man Who Made the Country.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Of the man who made the country, sing in eulo-
gistic phrases,
His patriot fame must never fade away.
He is fat, and blessed of fortune, and the daily
paper raises
Its voice to do him honour every day. y
He is clad in finest linen and in broadcloth sleek
and sable—
The purple is emblazoned in his phiz—
And he wears a wealth of diamonds and a watch-
chain like a cable—
The man who made the country what it is.
He laments o’er modern movements, like a fine,
old, crusted Tory,
And grieves to see the socialistic drift.
He is used to point a moral and adorn a little
story,
And he poses as a monument of Thrift.
s' , ,
He s the stamp, admirers tell us, of the noblest
nation-makers—
A Trojan in the pioneering fight;
He was here in eighteen-forty, and he grabbed the
fertile acres,
And dummied like a hero left and right. /
Whilst the greed of gain drove others out to delve
for golden gravel
This patriot was faithful to the soil,
And he watched Ins pastures widen and his rest-
less fences travel
O’er lull and dale, encompassing the spoil.
Never toiled he, and he spun not, but he held the
lands, and labour
Was suppliant and grateful to its lord,
And tbe workers in the cities and each sweating
nearer neighbour
Was shorn, like other sheep, to swell his
hoard.
Now he flaunts it on the block, and twenty men
of lowly station
Might live on what he pays away for fizz ;
He’ s an M.L C., a churchman, and a bulwark of
the nation
The man who made the country what it is.
On the great, grey plains the lost wind wanders,
east and westward blowing
The restless dust of dead men fame disowns,
The pioneers who forward to the front were ever
going,
And paving us a pathway with their bones.
And the men who cleft the bush through, and who
hewed the hills asunder,
Who knit the iron thews, and laid the way,
Who o’erthrew the rock-bound mountains, and
who spanned the rivers under,
And set the engines going—where are they ?
Where are they who to the nether hells went down
to beard the devil
And robbed him of his ruddy, boarded gold;
And the men who wrought the roads, and they
who laid the forests level,
And bade the soil its secret gifts unfold ?
Where the sun-browned, bearded swagmen, bred j
on labour and privation, |
By whom the sure foundation here was laid ?
Have we given all the glory to the men who made
the nation,
Or to those bloated men the nation made?
EDWARD DYSON.

tition No. 10 closes on Wednesday, Jan. 24, 1894.
Miss Sutherland, the Melb. philanthropist,
asked why she supported the Knorr reprieve,
said the babies were mostly born half dead.
Two bitterly rival Central Q. newspapers
ordered their Christmas supplements from the
South. Wicked rumour says that one of the pro-
prietors “ got at ” the bagman taking the order to
offer the opposition a certain supplement cheap.
The bait took, and the supplement (duly heralded
by glowing announcements of new and original
stories) was issued. In a paragraph teeming with
irony the up-to-date proprietor pointed out that
his loathsome contemporary was, as usual, some-
what behind the times, “Their Christmas supple-
ment is an exact copy of that issued by this paper
in 1892.”
“F.W.” sends the following indignant
communication:—
Dear Bulletin,— Under the heading, “In a Wet
Season," a most ridiculous article appeared in The Bul-
letin of Dec. 2. I can imagine the perpetrator; he
belongs to that class of journalists who do London or
Paris in a day, and then writes a few columns of insanity
to one of our colonial papers, tending to show how in-
ferior those cities are to Melb. or Sydney. The writer,
evidently, had gone broke in Bourke, and cadged a ticket
to travel on a sheep-train ; als ■, having a bad head and
boiled eyes, the result of (libellous expressions omitted
here), vented his spleen on the country. The “small,
hopeless-looking man ’’ he writes about, very likely earns
more money, honourably, in a week than (first-class libel
cutout.) The population of Byrock never look dismal;
in rainy weather joy is to be seen reflected on every face.
The mean, insulting remarks of H.L. in reference to
swagmen are beneath notice ; should he ever be “on the
road ” and needed help, not one of those men would
refuse to share their last crust with him. The place
“ where there was a rotten, weatherboard pub.” is not to
be found “ along the line.” There is a place where there
are two large, new hotels; one of them is a two-storey
brick building, and would have astonished H.L. had he
been .... to have seen the premises; the horses
seen about the pubs, are strong, well-fed animals; the
men, gathered about the substantial bar, are healthy and
open-handed. Judging from the previous efforts of H.L.
1 had an idea that he was a sort of Australian Bret Ilarte';
but, now, I imagine him an old, weak, vengeful creature
(actionable language reluctantly cut out.)

Harper’s Bazar is on the track of a new
social trouble, “ facial deshabille :
Does it not seem a thousand pities that more women
are not alive to the positive indecency of appearing at the
breakfast board in what maybe called facial deshabille?
Hardly anyone but considers her morning toilet complete
when she has done with tub and dumb-bells, “fixed”
her hair, and put on her dress ; yet she should never
venture beyond the threshold of her bedroom until she
has coaxed a smooth and smiling reflection into
her looking-glass. To do this we must first “de-
vitalise” the face; that is, cast out the unlovely
expression in possession by encouraging a sen-
sation of droop throughout the features, so that
cheeks, lips, chin and lids may sag heavily while the mind
is, as far,as possible, a blank. After “sozzling” in this
slate of relaxation for a few minutes, let us summon up a
pleasing mental picture, and then allow a little smile, 1
rippling over the face from opening eyes and parting lips,
to gradually bring the fed tires into place and expression.
If one is afflicted with obstinate facial dejection or
asperity, this operation should be repeated several times.
Hterthis, when we hear a dull thud upstairs in
the early morning, we will know it is the landlady
letting her features “ droop ” and devitalising
herself, preparatory to calling up a pleasing ex-
pression with which to come down to breakfast. 1

DOMESTIC ECONOMY!
’ WBCA.T IS IT ?
DOMESTIC ECONOMY is primarily the art of feeding and clothing the family at as low a rate as possible,
'without impairing the family digestion, or taking away the character of its members for being well and com-
fortably dressed. Domestic eoonomy is not comprised in buying a bad article for sixpence when better can be
got for sevenpence ; but if the ruling price for the best is eightpence, and somebody comes along and offers it for
sevenpenoe, then it’s domestic economy to close with the merchant so offering. By all means study this great
and glorious science of social life, because its due and proper observance cannot fail to make poor people rich, and
rich ones richer. What say you ? Will you do your business at
