The Wild Cat Column.
The outline of the proposed United States tariff
is to hand, and the reason why President Cleveland failed
to respond to that historic cablegram from the Free-
-11 aders of Sydney, or why, if he did respond, his reply
was judiciously suppressed, is apparent. The Democratic
tariff bill puts a few—but only a few—raw materials on
iliefree list—and apart from that its general tendency is to
reduce the old 60 per cent, duties to 40 per cent. There-
to e, the Freetrade tariff which the Parkes-Reid party is
<• leering about in the U.S. is, on an average, just three
i linos more protective than the Protectionist tariff which
I ie same party is cursing about in N.S.W. The famous
i-ablegram was all right in itself, but its destination was
wrong. It should have been sent to Dibbs instead of
Cleveland.
• « •
The Federal Building Society continues the
Peaceful course of its liquidation under the supervision of
.■■ie old director and the old manager—the third liquidator,
who was also an old director, having resigned. This
custom of entrusting the management under which an
i istitution vra« wrecked with the work of winding it up
seems incurable. In any place but Melbourne the fact
that a certain set of men had run a prosperous concern to
s nash woull he a sufficient reason for getting rid of them
and employing an altogether new crowd, who—if they
did nothing else—might at least give an unbiassed ex-
planation as to what the old crowd had been about; but,
in Melbourne, the old, gone-bung directorate seems to in-
herit the liquidation job as a matter of course.
The Federal Society badly needs re-construction.
During the year it has wiped out some £90,000 worth of
liabilities, and apparently it will be able, in another year
or two, to realise almost all its assets except the great
mass of at present unsaleable land and house-property; and
pay off a good proportion of its deposits. Then it must
either sell the aforesaid property at a loss of about 75 per
cent., or else sit down peacefully and wait until the next
boom enables it to realise ; and pay the big salaries of the
two liquidators—which will gradually eat up the society’s
prospects—while it is waiting. If the society is started
again it should at least earn enough to pay salaries—
which are just about the same in a live institution as in a
dead one ; meanwhile, it earns nothing.
The utter absurdity of the last reconstruction
scheme almost suggests, however, that there is no special
desire to get the Federal Society afloat again. If it does
start once more it should begin with entirely new man-
agement and a new directorate, and nobody related to
James Munro, or any of his friends, in even the fortieth
degree of consanguinity, should be allowed inside the
office door. As a thoroughly serious Scotch Jonah,
Munro has no equal in Australia.
* » •
The re-constructed Yic. banks, having bluffed
off their own creditors, are sitting down heavily on the
handful of land-boomers who still survive. This year will
probably' see the veiy l ist boomer of them all put through
the mill for the customary 6d. or so in the £. A year or
so back the banks were disposed to deal tenderly with the
old wrecks, and give them time, and do their best to tide
them over, under the impression that when times grew
better they would rise to renewed wealth and become
valuable customers once more.
But this feeling is now pretty nearly dead.
The boom will come again, no doubt, but when it
does there will be a new generation of boomers, and
the present crowd will mostly be old and greasy
and unshaven and nasally carbuncled, and their frowsy
hair will stick up through their shattered hats, and they
will breathe stale beer at the free-lunch counter while
they swap lies about their vanished greatness. A few in-
domitable spirits will come to the front a second time and
float banks with all their former affability, but, as a general
rule, each boom brings a new crowd to the front, and the
old crowd mostly takes refuge in the Soup Kitchen.
Therefore the banks are seemingly going to sell
the balance of the whited sepulchres for what they will
fetch, and get rid of them. Taking them all round, they
won’t fetch much.
* • m
Queensland’s great experiment of making the
note-issue a State monopoly and abolishing the paper
money of the banks is working well up to date. How
long it will work is a matter of serious doubt. The
Queensland Fat Man Government, with its constitutional
inability to go straight, went crooked on this occasion, as
usual. The whole financial policy of the Government,
no miller what devious track it may take, has always
the Sanaa goal—the interests of the Q.N. Bank ; and even
the seemingly progressive idea of making the currency a
State, instead of a private, monopoly' gets there just the
same. Tne Queensland notes are legal tender throughout
the country, and can be exchanged for gold at the
Treasury, where a 25 per cent, reserve is kept in
sovereigns. But the notes are circulated in a fearful and
wonderful plan. When any bank wants £lOO,OOO worth of
paper it deposits £25,000 in sovereigns with the Govern-
ment and gets it. The remaining £75,000 is credited
to the Government as a deposit on which a nominal
rate of interest is paid.
In case of a panic, the whole .£lOO,OOO in notes
might arrive at the Treasury with a demand for gold.
Therefore, the State would have to find £lOO,OOO in gold at
an instant’s notice, w hereas it had only received one-fourth
of the amount, the other three-fourths being owed to it
by the bank without any security. And though the State
has to pay the whole amount on the instant, it can only
call on the bank to pay in four instalments extending over
nine months.
Therefore, the situation stands thus. A bank
doing a large business, and in straitened circumstances,
might lodge £500,000 in gold at the Treasury and get
£2,000,000 in State notes, which are quite as service-
able. It credits the Government with £1,500,000 as
a deposit, and, consequently, getsa loan of thisamount on
no security. It may be utterly and hopelessly insolvent,
but that makes no difference ; its position is not inquired
into. If there is no national panic and no extensive
demand for gold at the Treasury, it. keeps on owing the
deposit and floating new loans on a similar basis as long
as the bank lasts. And as no bank need break so long as
it can find a sovereign to leave with the Treasury and get
£4 in notes in exchange, it follows that a bank may go on
getting more and more dry-rotted through a long course
of years while showing no outward sign of decay, and
when the smash does come it will be a sure sign that
nothing remains, and that ail the millions owing to the
Government are gone with the rest.
A State note-issue without a State Bank is a
hazardous device. The Queensland system is simply a
scheme whereby any hank which is in difficulties can
stave off trouble by getting the loan from the State
of paper money, for the redemption of which the State is
responsible. And all the security the State possesses is
the right to call for repayment of the loan by four instal-
ments. Which is not so very much more solid than the
right to call on Jabez Balfour to come back from Argentina
and settle up with his victims; or to call on spirits to
arise out of the vasty deep, or the blessed privilege of
calling on bread to drop from heaven, or of going out
into a lonely place and calling generally.
* • •
Huddart, Parker and Co., which lately resumed
its Maoriland steamship service with the new s.s. Tas-
mania, still prospers, and keeps up a fierce competition
with the powerful Union Co. But ships whose names
h"gin with a “T” have an evil reputation for ill-luck.
The Union Co. had bad fortune with the same ill-fated
letter, and the bones of at least three Union vessels that
carried it lie about the Maoriland coast to this day.

Rumoured that a large Melb. firm will “go”

In one of the largest banks in Sydney there are
25 adult clerks with salaries under £2 per week I
A Sydney suburban storekeeper who sold out a
large business 12 months ago, believing he possessed an
assured life-independence, was tempted into gold-mining,
and figured last week among the applicants for out-door
dispensary relief in Sydney Domain.
* • •
Broken Hill (N.S.W.) Knox says that the price
of silver is regulated privately from day to day in London
by two or three firms who act as brokers and dealers.
“As a matter of fact, three gentlemen, representatives
of very excellent and reputable firms, meet at the office
of one of their number every afternoon, and, after a short
conversation as to the stocks of silver coming forward,
&c., say, ‘ Well, the price to-day shall be so-and-so.’ The
amount decided upon is then written and affixed to a
board, and the thing is done. If there were an open
market we should be able, of course, to sell our silver on
the same terms as govern other transactions, but there is
nothing of the kind.” So that three London gentlemen
actually caused the last Broken Hill strike! Mr. Knox, who
ought to know his way about, declares that he found this
combination too powerful to do much with.
* * •
At time of going to press Walsh, Q.C., on
behalf of the Victorian Land Boom Government, was
“considering” the papers in the Mercantile Bank case
and endeavouring to decide whether the case should go
to a jury or not. Apparently, after two years’ hard, con-
sistent thought over the Mercantile case, there is still as
much room for “consideration” as there ever was, and
the question whether there will be any trial after all is
the same absolutely unbiased toss-up as before. The Law
Department is so absolutely unprejudiced that it would
seriously sit down to consider the papers in the case of
Adam without even noticing that the freshness had gone
off the subject a good deal, and that it had got past the
stage where abstract consideration could do it any good.
* • *
In N.S.W. financial circles the Chinese are
regarded as commercially more honest than any other
section of the community.

The New Oriental liquidator thinks the total
dividend will amount to 12s. 6d. instead of 15s.
• • •
The latest report of Manager Begelhole, of
Bayley’s Reward claim, reads like a dream of Eldorado.
No. 1 cutting, the site of Bayley’s first discovery: reef
opened up 108 ft. to a depth of 15ft.; stone averaging 7ft.
wide. Raised from this block 650 tons, of which 12 tons
picked stone gave 8500 oz. smelted gold. To further test
value of lode, he sank inclined shaft 80ft. deep; lode
very regular all the way down and Oft. wide. Raised 160
tons in sinking shaft, picked out four tons which gave
1600 oz. gold. At 50ft. opened out drives north and south.
Lode in north drive sft.; 90 tons raised in which gold was
frequently seen; south drive, 90ft., lode 6ft. wide: 250
tons broken out. Five tons, picked, gave 2000 oz. smelted
gold. Over back of this drive he has sloped a small piece
of ground for 70 tons ; picked out 2 jtons for 900 oz. So
much for No. 1 cutting.
No. 2 cutting is not so rich. It is 100 ft. nort^ l
■of No. 1 and opened out 50ft. long and 15ft. deep. Lod e
Bft. wide ; 200 tons broken, four tons picked for IOOOoz-
Shaft going down in floor of cutting: present depth, 32ft.,
showing rich stone all the way. Sixty tons raised; 35cwt.
picked stone gave SOOoz. Stone in bottom very rich,
and heavy yields expected here; hence shaft is being
■carried down to 100 ft. ere opening out.
Mr. Begelhole continues his dream-like report.
In lease 180, bought and added to the Reward mine, a
cutting has been made 60ft. long, eight feet deep, lode
eight feet tliick, shaft, sunk in the cutting, 70ft. deep ;
raised SOOoz. from 25cwt. picked stone. About 300 ft.
north of shaft he has put a cut across lode, which is there
20ft. wide but comparatively poor. After referring to
the gold-bearing character of another reef, known as the
Western, from which 100 tons has been broken, the
manager proceeds to deal with ore reserves.
About 1700 tons have been broken from the
different workings, which the manager estimates to go at
least 6 ozs. per ton. Then there are 100 tons wash-dirt
cut from surface in dam-making; prospects equal to
2 ozs. Also, 600 tons of surface, which seems to be the
overflow from the lode, “and which is so rich in gold that
ozs. can sometimes be picked out of it in a few minutes.
Recent tests have proved the value to be over 10oz. per ton.
You will note we have at grass to-day, ready for the
battery, 2500 tons, which 1 estimate to yield over 16,000
ozs.”
“Going below, we have the rich block of
ground between the floors of No. 1 cutting and the back
of the south drive from the 50ft. level. This block is 30ft.
high, and will, without doubt, be quite as rich as any-
thing either in driving or cutting. The block contains
1200 tons, and, as the stone both over and under has
yielded 18oz. per ton, I think we are justified in assuming
the block will maintain the same average, which would
mean a total of 21,0000 z. At No. 2 cutting we have below
the floor 300 tons in sight, worth 17oz. per ton. So that,
in round numbers, we may say there are 40,0000 z. in
sight down to the 50ft. level only. The incline shaft is
down 30ft. below that, and as the stone is quite as good
in the bottom of the drive as that now showing in the
back, I am of opinion that when the 100 ft. level is reached
we will open up another 50ft. block equal in richness to
the one now in work. There is also a very large quantity
of payable stone in sight on XBO lease and on the western
reef."
Was there ever such a mine as that sold for
£6000! The writer met Mr. Gorrie, the original owner of
Bayley’s Reward South, a few days ago. Gorrie is a
practical miner, and was asked to point out where in his
report manager Begelhole had exaggerated. He replied:
“There is no exaggeration. I have seen the mine and
know the man, and I can say that he has under-estimated
a good deal. For instance, he reckons the Western reef
at 2ozs. per ton ; while I know it will go fully saz. We
have opened the reef up in the South mine, and from
numerous prospects taken from the reef in both properties
I am certain it will go what 1 say.”
• I * •
Items from the report on the Murchison reefs
by a man who has been on most Australian fields:—“ln
six months Daly and party have dollied 1600 oz.; about
same quantity has been taken out of next claim. Austin
Island, considering its size, is the richest spot yet dis-
covered in W. A. The first reef on the Island was found
by Lawrence and Mooney who dollied out 600 oz. In the
next claim Regan and party say they dollied about 6000 oz.
in 18 months. I saiw one parcel of stone in Pearce’s claim
out of which SOOOok. have been taken. When I left they
were also dollying stone from the Goloonda and expected
to get IOOOoz. in a week. The Evening Star claim is
phenomenally rich, and already SOOOoz. have been dollied.
On the whole, I don’t hesitate the opinion that the Mur-
ohison will prove a successful rival to Ooolgardie.’ If so,
we ought to hear ten times as much about it.

The trend of affairs in Block 10 mine is shown
by comparing the silver production during the first ana
last halves of the past year. For the first half it was
1,006,878 ozs. ; for the last, 369,000 ozs.
* * *
Broken Hill silver vield for 1893 was 15,922,334
ozs.; lead, 71,991 tons. ail. Proprietary gave 12,459,765
ozs. silver, 47,758 tons lead ; Block 10, 1,370,878 ozs.
silver ; Block 14. 935,820 ozs. ; no other mine got over the
half million. The yield is a record one. In ’9l it was
15,650,000 ozs. ; in ’92 (strike year) 13,550,(00 ozs.
Relatively small quantities of copper and gold were
recovered as by-products.
• # •
Reported that on the Wadham lease a reef
has been hit 40ft. down, believed to be the richest dis-
covered at Murchison, and to vie with Bayley's. There
is no idea yet of the size of the body of stone, but its
richness is apparent from the specimens, in which the
gold is simply held together by the quartz. Thirteen
batteries or mills are either in full swing, in course of
erection, or are on the way to the Murchison.
» # •
Two N.Q. miners have arrived at Ooolgardie
after al3 months’ walk across the continent. Their per-
formance is mentioned in a bald, four-line telegram, and
after that it will probably drop out of sight and notice.
It is only such “ great explorers ” as Calvert from Eng-
land, who travel along a main road w’ith a huge commis-
sariat department lumbering behind them, and then go
“Home ” to fill up the gaping London editor, who get big
“ ads." in the papers and become famous.

Up to June, ’93, the Broken Hill Proprietary
mine had paid £6,669,000 in dividends.
• • •
Another mountain of gold is vaguely reported—
from Maoriland. A gum-digger, struck with the curious
formation of Parahaki mount, at Whangarei, dug up at
random three samples of the hill and sent them for assay.
Alleged results, 60oz. per ton. Usual syndicate incu-
bating.
# • •
An enquiry for Vic. coal has just reached Mel-
bourne from Westralia. Which possibly means that
ex-Premier Munro begins once more to feel his financial
way.
• # •
Efforts are once more being made to re-open
Euriowie (N.S.W.) tinfleld. A syndicate, of which J. S.
Reid is a member, has bought one of the many boom tin-
dressing plants, and is now engaged in prospecting adja-
cent properties—the leases of most of which can be got
for a mere song. The crowd interested is not given to
throwing away its money.
• • *
Gympie mines are indeed the best-managed on
earth. The Calton Hill Freehold claim was long out of
luck. Shareholders wearied of paying calls. So the mine
was hung up, and tools and stock sold. After paying
liabilities the directors had on hand a £lO balance ; with
it they bought 20 shares in Tatts.’s sweep on the Carring-
ton Stakes. They drew Stanmore, who won ; shareholders
have now about £2600 nett to divide. Who talks of the
decay of the mining industry ?
* * •
Editor Bulletin, — Coolgardie fairly “ reeks ”
with men representing three-legged syndicates and
writing their experiences to the papers. They go in
for big leases and plenty of beer. The public will have
to pay for all by-and-bye if they don’t look out. —
Fqssicker, Perth.

The Illawarra district (N.S.W.) coal-strike is
purely a defensive campaign. It is a struggle for self-
preservation against absolute starvation. It is, in moral
reality, an employers’ lock-out, and not a miners’ strike.

A.S.U., has invented a machine that unanswerably
settles whether wool is “wet," or merely “greasy."

Manly branch of the Sydney Commercial Bank
has been closed, and the building is to be used as an
hotel. Actual liquid-ation.
* # •
At Coolgardie, says a Bulletin correspondent,
there is little gold actually getting, except at Bayley’s,
and some duffer-reefs are being floated. There is, in
fact, a reef-fever. Men talk about their 12 or 30-acre
gold-leases as though they were millionaires. Not a
reefer but has a claim better than Bayley’s—and he will
often finish his story by asking you to lend him half-a-
sov. or half-a-crown.
• • •
The Sydney assayers are at last formally pro-
testing against the assay branch of N.S.W. Mines Dept,
doing private work firatis and wholesale. Within Tub
Bulletin’s knowledge the Department has been assaying
Queensland quartz for Sydney speculators, who were too
mean to pay an assayer his fee.

Tilden’s mine has reached the forfeited-share
Stage. Battery going up. Shares going down.
A public crushing-battery is as good as a gold-
mine In W.A. At Bar £2 10s. a ton is charged
for crushing, and £2 for lots over 100 tons.

career has put through over 230,000 tons for 173,3300 z.
gold, value £601,448, of which £201,700 has been paid in
diva. For years it averaged nearly an ounce, then
14dwts., afterwards 10, and during the last few months
only 7dwts.

After many years of call-paying, the Brilliant
Extended has struck the true fissure of the Brilliant mine
at 2040 ft. The reef shows splendid gold, and is over 6ft.
wide. This is the result of £lB,OOO spent in the right
direction. The co. to perform it was formed in Adelaide
in ’9l; since then most of the Adelaideans have tired and
quitted their interests to Towers men. More deep shafts
will follow.

Sydney D.T.’s article on the Bingera (N.S.W.)
diamond-mines has innocently nobbled the Pall Mall
Gazette, which reprints an illustration of the Monte
Oristo mine, as well as the portrait of the octogenarian,
Capt. Rogers, with several astounding additions of its
own. “ The Monte Cristo mine itself is a veritable moun-
tain of diamonds, pronounced by experts to be the richest
in the world.” A good start, that. De Beers (with a
market value of £12,000,000) will now, probably, be set
down as next to Monte Cristo. “ Its output must influ-
ence, if not govern, the diamond market. Should the •
matrix prove as rich as the surrounding country indicates
the mine will be a kingdom." Again we are told that the
Rothschilds are after the captain, whose great age and
partial blindness will prevent his keeping ahead of them.

&c., Quiz. Sunny Corner, 6/l/’94. [The success-

by no means honestly impossible streak of good

many are in the habit of doing so. It was abso-
lutely impossible for any competitor to be “ in the
know.”— Manager B.]
The Macquarie groans at the
New Year’s look-out. The publicans in one town-
ship there, a few days before Xmas, petitioned
the police to clear the river-banks of travellers,
lest the latter should meander up in force for the
usual free Xmas dinner. A squatter on the river
has shifted his “travellers’ hut” to a place sur-
rounded by five or six ant-beds, populated with
the “meat-ant.” A station-hand fell off a dray
and got killed. Next day, 16 men crowded up to
the station with one burden—“ Any chance of a
job, boss?” “No, full up.” “How's that?
Wasn’t there a bloke killed here yesterday ?”
Bathurst burrs are scarce on the Macquarie this
year, which is the greatest calamity of all for
travellers.

That dreary moral reflection “ murder
will out ” has seen its best days. In the ten years
ending 1886 there were 1766 known murders in
England and Wales, and 1094 of them have never
been cleared up to this day, nor has any trace of the
murderer been found. When allowance is made
for all the unknown murders—those where the
body itself is never discovered, and the matter is
merely set down as a mysterious disappearance,
and for all the murders which are successfully
covered by medical certificates, and those which
pass as suicide or death by accident, and those
shady cases where the body is buried without any
medical certificate at all—in one county, 42 per
cent, of the deaths are not verified by any doctor,
and the deceased may have his head cut off, or
have 21b. of strychnine in him for all the law
knows to the contrary—it is probable that only
one British murderer in ten is ever found.

The writing of the Caligraph can always be recognised
by its peculiar evenness and regularity of impression ;
this is due to its being the only typewriter which w rites
on a flat surface.
The Electric Telegraph Department of N.S. Wales, after
testing every machine in the market, has just given the
Agent for the Caligraph the largest order ever given in
Australia in one line for Typewriters.
There are now 120 Caligraphs in the Government
Departments of N.S. Wales. There are more Caligraphs
in the public offices of Australia than machines of all
other makes combined.
The Caligraph will manifold 20 copies at one operation
by interleaving carbon, and when used in conjunction
with Edison’s Mimeograph will produce 1500 copies from
one stencil, each equal to the best original work. Send
for Mimeograph circular.
The New Special No. 3 Caligraph las two interchange-
able platens, a lateral ribbon movement, and six addi-
tional keys which may be fitted with fractions or other
characters to order.

A Lamb to the Slaughter i A Story of
Bornco.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Sabi wanted a head. That wasn’t much.
Dyak girls often did want a head from their
suitors. But Sabi was a belle—her father was
worth' many brass cannon* —and the head Sabi
vvanted was a white man’s !
Now, white men were scarce in the south-west,
and their heads were dangerous to get—single-
handed. So Achang chewed betel over the pro-
blem for a full hour, and then, being a man of
action, took his weapons and went over to Panda
the blacksmith.
Panda was a Kayan—despised as a man, but
mightily respected as a sword-maker. Also, he
could put two and two together. The price of
Sabi’s hand he knew, and knew, too, who was the
only one of her half-hundred admirers likely to
pay it. So when he saw the silk surong and the
leopard-skin jacket his fish-like mouth twisted
knowingly up towards his overloaded ear.
Silently he received the Dyak dandy’s swords.
Kris and ihlang he put aside, then took up the
latok.
“This wants no help from me,” he said.
“Never was a better edge!” and then, with his
eye on Achang’s, he made a lightning sweep with
the sword, and stooped as if to pick up with his
left hand some object from the ground. The
blood-lust flashed into Achang’s inky eyes, and
the muscles stiffened up under his saddle-coloured
skin, as. for an answer, he rubbed his hand rapidly
over his cheek and chin.
Panda understood. A beard ! —and therefore a
white man.
“ Good !” he said. “ Thou climbest high. Is it
Stobsi whose neck is to melt ?—Stobsi, the stick-
hunter ?”
“No ! thou Kayan pig ! Stobsi is as one of our
own people. I would myself take his head who
would take Stobsi’s ! Besides, fool, hath Stobsi a
beard ? Scrapeth he not his face every day with
a little parang till it is smooth as a shark’s belly ?”
Now Stubbs, the “stick-hunter,” was not loved
of Panda. The blacksmith, like many a Kayan,
dispensed with a waistcloth because he had a pair
of drawers tattooed upon his dingy hide—at which
thing the little cockney had laughed hugely. But
to laugh at a Kayan is to make a deadly enemy,
and Panda was ingenuously sorry that Stubbs’
was not the head to fall. Now, too, that Achang
had called him a pig, he had to get level also with
that turmeric-tinted warrior, and as he blew
sullenly at his charcoal he saw how.
“ If it isn’t Stobsi,” he told himself, “it must
be Bekkul; no other white men within twelve
days’ journey.” And Bekkul —otherwise Beck-
ley—had a fast friend in Kamsut, the Tuan of
Rubianak.
Panda dropped his bellows and brought out a
jar of tuak. Only at set feasts do the Dyaks, as a
rule, get drunk. Still, there are exceptions, and
Achang was one of them ; so the evil-smelling
stuff, having gone liberally down his throat, got
duly up into his head. Then he babbled, and
Panda lay low for him. Bekkul was to be the
victim, and Achang, with drunken circumstanti-
ality, set forth his plan of operations. Then, as
tuak-drinkers will, he fell incontinently fast
asleep, whereupon Panda, girding up his loins
with an unwonted waistclout, slipped unostenta-
tiously into the jungle and started eastwards.
# • •
To Beckley, two days later, there sud-
denly appeared, as the result of Panda’s little
excursion, the Tuan of "Rubianak himself, with a
tail of 50 Malays and one unwilling Chinaman.
“ Ask no questions, Bekkul, my father,” said
Kamsut. “Change now thy garments and give
to these men those thou wearest.”
Beckley, who knew his man, obeyed without a
word, and wondered much to see his clothes placed
upon the no less astonished Chow. The Tuan took
out one of his watches —he wore three ! —then
looked at the sinking sun.
“At dusk of every evening, Bekkul, thou
drawest water by the reed-bed yonder; but to-
night goeth down this yellow dog in thy stead.
Why—thou shaltin due time understand ! ”
Down went the sun, and down went Sun Ling
to the water, in Beckley’s rig, and with Beckley’s
jars. At the margin of the reedy wall he put
down his vessels, and was stooping to admire the
reproduction of his yellow phiz, surmounted by
Beckley’s topee, when the reeds parted gently be-
side him, and, plunge!—reflection and reflected
came suddenly together, as Sun Ling’s shaven
sconce swept clean from his shoulders —flopped
sullenly into the green-black water ! The slayer
threw down his parang, dropped a knee upon the
red-spouting neck, and picked out of the water
the still fatuously grinning head. Then, as he
stood astounded, came running from the hut the
Tuan’s men, spreading out to cut him off.
Achang looked, snatched up his parang, threw
it down again, and bounded far into the darkening
river.
With fifty strokes he bottomed on the further
side and turned to see how many followed him.
Not one !—for now from beneath the hollow bank
behind him there stole swiftly out a mighty ripple
and a long, grey snout came towards the wader
with a rush. There was a shriek that soared to
wing again a cloud of roosting copper-birds ; then
Achang’s brass-ringed ankles flourished for a
second in the air as his body was crushed! in the
five-foot jaws that took him under like a frog.
ALEX. MONTGOMERY.
* Brass-guns— two and three pounders—constitute a
B ort o( standard of value among the Dyaks.

Retrenchment.
[FOR THE BULLETIN].
Tis a time of deep depression, there’s retrench-
ment in the air ;
There’s a cutting-down of prices and of wages
everywhere;
There’s a turning of old garments, and a patching
of old pants,
And a tendency to cotton to one’s wealthy maiden
aunts.
People say that we began it, bub we really needn’t
mind,
For it isn’t to this planet that the trouble is con-
fined :
It’s a kind of epidemic, and, I’ve recently been
told,
They’re retrenching up in Heaven, and econo-
mising gold.
Even there the horrid poison the celestial ether
taints;
They are cutting up old rainbows into haloes for
the saints;
The harps which once were golden are nob golden
any more,
And they’re serving out tin-whistles to the angels
by the score.
The pearly gates have vanished, and a pearl you
never see;
And amethysts are scarcer there than lawyers
used to be.
The cherubim are wearing leather slippers on
their feet,
And they’re substituting metal for the gold upon
the street.
There’s Saint Peter in a fury, for they’ve got his
golden key—
He nearly used an adjective commencing with a
“D”-
He fastens up the portals, and it goes against his
grain,
With a common kind of padlock and an ordinary
chain.
Then there’s Abraham, and Moses, and the ancient
Jewish lot —
Well, they’ve got a little “ corner,” and intend to
make a “ pot.”
The Hebrew kings, who used to frighten subjects
with their frowns,
Are “ advancing ” to the seraphs on the jewels in
their crowns!
It’s an awful kind of crisis, and a pretty state of
things,
When an impecunious cherub has to pop his harp
and wings,
But it’s worse when weary angels can’t sit down and
rest their bones
’Gos a “ring ” of Hebrew prophets has a lien upon
their thrones.
What is looming in the future ? What is coming ?
Who can tell
That the trouble’s not extending to the other
place as well T
Don’t imagine, gentle reader, that you’ll find it,
when you go,
Any better, one iota, in the regions down below.
’Cos it’s rather more than likely, I imagine, on
the whole,
They’re retrenching there in sulphur, and econo-
mising coal;
Instead of grilling pleasantly, in privacy and
ease,
You may have to share a gridiron with a lot of
C.M.G.’s.

thoughtful.

Oolemane & Sons’ Special Eucal. Oil is A one.
More trouble looms ahead for Senor Lane’
The “ Disciples of Christ ” are sending from Mel*
bourne a missionary to the New Australia colony
in Paraguay. The members of the sect believe
that “ without baptism by immersion there is not
salvation,” and they propose to interrupt the
urgent bush-clearing operations by laying this
view of the matter before the New Australians at
full length.
Oolemane & Sons’ Eucalypte Pills stir one up.
A story is going round the Australian
papers—and has been going round for some weeks
—concerning a beautiful Irish boy who lately
suicided in Dublin. The tale is partly a new
version of the Boy who stood on the Burning Deck
and partly a horrid parody on “Little Jim.”
This awful youth—aged 12—was of a wealthy
family, but his parents became so much reduced
in circumstances that he and his father and
mother and his four little brothers and sisters had
to live in one small room, which ws,s also occupied
by three other families. If the, three other
families were of the same size, this makes nearly
30 people inhabiting that one small room, and if
any of the families took in boarders that would
further aggravate the evil. This looks pretty
“steep”—but, no matter. Then the 12-year-old
son of this reduced family resolved to sacrifice
himself for the good of his parents. He scraped
up 4s. and spent it in the purchase of various
comic papers and other things which have
coupons attached guaranteeing payment of large
amounts if a person is found dead with the goods
in his possession. He got together coupons to the
amount of £3500, and then he left a pathetic letter
for his mother—and drowned himself, so that she
might get the money. And—here the full horror
of the tale comes in—the name of this small boy
was “Ferdinand de Freyne de Courcy.”
He signed it in full at the end of the pathetic
letter which he left for his mother. That name
and the story of the four families that lived in
one small room ought to break the narrative up,
but the dunderheaded Australian dailies keep on
publishing it in all seriousness, and probably won’t
begin to see the joke until it further transpires
that the name of one of the other three families
was Hernando Oaulainoourt Rohan Gonzales de
Montmorency von Alvenaleben. Then it will
gradually dawn on them that there is something
wrong. How the Irish gentleman who wrote that
paragraph must grin !
Oolemane & Sons’ Eucal. Loz. are for the voice
Pastor Fitchett, in Southern Cross, asks
why the newspaper gives a column to a circus,
patronised by 1000 people, and only 20 lines to a
religious meeting attended by 2000 people. The
reason is because the religious world doesn’t pay
for news, but cribs all the godly papers in the
Athenaeums, so that the Southern Cross has to
appeal with piteous whines.
Dear Bulletin, — I found the following in
a recent paper :
The most urgently-needed reform in Australia is the
abolition of the British black suit and the British black
top hat. The hat never served any useful purpose ; it is
hard, ungainly, and as difficult to carry about as the
remains of a murdered relative. As for the suit it is as
unsuited to the climate as a vest of chain armour with a
gridiron helmet. For eight months in the year white
cotton or linen is the only fitting wear in Australia.
I wish to object to this; the statements look
feasible, but even the cool white linen suit has its
disadvantages. I owned one for about a month
and found it the most disastrous outfit on record.
The trouble began in a very far north Queensland
town—a sweltering, ant-eaten, ghost-haunted,
snake-infested settlement, balanced on the outer
rim of perdition. An unemployed pressman came
to this town one day, and the proprietor of a small
monthly publication hired him aseditor, sub-editor,
reporter, manager, collector, horse-power and
general rouseabout. His engagement was short and
troubled. He went amissing the first day, and
the third day he was found drunk by the side of a
lagoon with a swelled head and a great deficiency
in his cash. His proprietor shook him up, and
in emphatic language gave him 24 hours to square
his accounts, whereupon he ran back to the town
in affright, gathered up as much of his baggage as
he could carry, gave the rest of his clothes to a
friend for safe-keeping lest they should be seized
in settlement of his hotel-bill, and then fell on
board the steamer and was seen in these parts no
more.
Then the friend, being of a treacherous turn,
proceeded to realise upon the clothes and drink
the proceeds, and among other transactions he
sold me a linen suit and cork helmet, taking a
sapphire scarf-pin in exchange. (I was also
leaving the town, by the way). There was a great
orgie up there in those days, and when he had
drunk most of the clothes and was just getting
through the umbrella and an overcoat he lost his
billet through trying to walk a chalk-line in the
office and betting six drinks that he could
throw a quantity of ink into the editor’s eye
through the keyhole. Finally, on the day of my
departure, he endeavoured to pawn the sapphire
pin, and discovered that it was glass. He
demanded restitution, and, not getting it, he fol-
lowed me down to the wharf, and there addressed
the town generally concerning my character.
Then, a bright idea occurring to his corrupted
intelligence, he ran back to the town and informed
my tailor and a few other disinterested friends
and creditors of my departure. They all streamed
down to the steamer, where they arrived too late
to interview me, but just in time to telegraph to
have me intercepted at the next port, where I
only escaped by concealing myself among the
coals. Finally, I reached Melbourne in safety,
and almost the first man I met was the original
owner of the linen suit and the cork helmet. He
demanded their return, with the alternative of
legal proceedings, and, under the circumstances, I
surrendered them. I have loathed the sight of
anything even distantly resembling them ever
since.—Yours, Titus Salt.
An Old Man’s Darling.
[FOR THE BULLETIN].
The skipper of the Mahina was such »
dear old man. Bright, English-red face, merrily-
laughing eyes of deepest blue, enshrined in a mop
of silvery-white hair ; age of body perhaps 65, of
heart 20 summers. He came to Apia from Hono-
lulu to try and sell a cargo of Puget Sound lumber,
aecordeons, and J.D.K.Z. gin.
• » •
A religious storekeeper brought him to tho
little Mission Church on the bank of the Vasigago,
one Sunday. The women looked with pleasant
smiles at the burly figure of the gay-hearted old
mariner ; the men with intense admiration at the
pretty girl who nestled beside him—his wife, a
native of the Hawaiian Islands. Such a lovely
little straight nose, soft, kissable mouth, and dark,
melting eyes. He looked like a Christianised
double of roving Bully Hayes; she likd an
equatorial Madonna with fluffy hair and grey kid
gloves.
# * •
“ How sweetly happy she looks!” sighfully
said a pretty white lady. “ I’m sure he must be a
loveable old man.” (Yet she had a husband, and
plenty of servants—just as pretty as the old cap-
tain’s wife). N.B.—lt’s the custom for married
white women to sigh in Polynesia. However, she
took the Hawaiian angel home to dinner.
* # #
At ten next morning the Mahina hove
short waiting for the captain to come aboard. He
came—joyously drunk. “Heave up, Growler!”
he said to the mate.”
“But Niaisn’t aboard, sir.”
“Look here, Growler” (with dignity), “you—
just—do—(hie)—as—l—tell—you. My wife’s all
right. Spent the eveningsh with Mr. and Mrs.
—. Promished be aboard ten sharp. Isn’t
here. If she ain’t aboard before we—(hie)—get
out o’ the passhage she’ll have to wait in Samoa
till I come back. Get the ship out o’ this—(hie) —
Growler. I must go and lay down a bit.”
“All right, sir. But here’s Mr. ’s boat
coming off now. Shall I heave her to outside?”
“Who else is in the boat ? ”
“Only Mr. ——.”
“Ah!”
* # *
“It’s no use, Nia, we can’t catch her,”
said the Hawaiian angel’s companion. “He must
be drunk. And you’ll get wet through, pet.”
He slewed the boat round, the crew set the sail,
and headed for the shore.
* # *
Mrs. promised to treat her like a
sister.
# * #
A few months afterwards Mr. got
a letter from the good old man :
San Francisco, June 10, 188-. Dear . Sorry I
couldn’t wait—breeze too stiff and I was drunk. Nia
would have been mad. She has my watch. Please send
ic me by Oppermann, of the Montiaro ;he knows me. I
won’t come back to Apia ; I lost money over that timber.
Nia is all right. I told Oppermann about her, so if she
wants to get back to Oahu you can take her to him.
Copra has gone down $7 a ton here. Tell Nia I hope she
didn’t get wet.—Yours fraternally, Thomas .
TALA MONI.

The Coolgardie gold boom is the only Australian boom for some years past
which shows signs of permanence. Coolgardie’s chief drawbacks are dust and heat
and myriads of flies, and the desert, variegated only by occasional
brackish mud-holes, which lies between it and the nearest civilisation. But the
gold is there, and gold is the one essential.
